==PAGE:5==
iii
Abstract
Many real-life problems involve multiple, conﬂicting objectives that must be optimized simulta-
neously while balancing diverse stakeholder preferences. Recent methods have been developed
to handle such complicated problems, combining Multi-Criteria Decision-Making (MCDM) and
Multi-objective Optimization Problems (MOP) methods. However, these methods often require
intensive cognitive eﬀorts from the stakeholders and can be computationally heavy.
As the
complexity of the decision problem increases, so do the limitations of the current methods.
This master’s thesis proposes the NIMBUS-ELECTRE Integrated Multi-Objective (NEIMO)
framework, which combines the strengths of the widely used synchronous NIMBUS and ELEC-
TRE TRI methods. The NEIMO algorithm is designed to dynamically adjust aspiration levels
and reclassify objectives based on stakeholders’ preferences, facilitating an iterative and ﬂexible
interactive decision-making process. The NEIMO framework is validated through a simulated
case study on the distribution problem of high school students in the capital region of Denmark.
The framework eﬀectively generated a set of Pareto optimal solutions, demonstrating its capabil-
ities to manage conﬂicting objectives while integrating stakeholder preferences. The case study
indicates that the NEIMO framework could improve decision-making outcomes by mitigating
cognitive burdens without compromising stakeholder satisfaction while ensuring adequate com-
putational performance. The success of the NEIMO framework in the case study demonstrates
its potential for broader applications in complex multi-objective optimization problems. Future
work should explore how to implement the framework in a real-life case, how to reduce objective
dimensionality, and how to evaluate its performance.

Chapter 1
Introduction
Possibly, since the dawn of humanity, people have been intrigued by the complexities of decision-
making and the principles guiding how decisions should be made. Therefore, identifying the best
decisions has been one of the perpetual intellectual challenges in engineering and science [1].
This challenge is even more relevant in today’s modern society, as addressing real-life problems
often encompasses multiple stakeholders with varying perspectives and multiple objectives to
be optimized simultaneously. A ﬁtting example of such a problem is the distribution of high
school students in the Capital Region of Denmark. This particular problem has garnered much
political attention in recent years and poses diﬀerent perspectives and multiple objectives that
must be optimized simultaneously.
As the decision-making process becomes more intricate, eﬀective and eﬃcient decision-making
approaches for handling real-life problems are needed.
One of the best-known classes of methods within decision-making is the research ﬁeld of Multi-
Criteria Decision-Making (MCDM) [1]. The ﬁeld has grown considerably over the past decades,
encompassing various application areas, as new methods are developed and old ones are improved
[2]. Central to the methods within this ﬁeld is including decision-makers preferences in assessing
a pre-deﬁned set of alternatives. However, as the size of the decision-making problem increases,
so does the complexity of including decision-makers and evaluating the alternatives.
Another well-known and ever-evolving research ﬁeld is Multi-Criteria Optimization. This ﬁeld
addresses a distinct class of problems, Multi-Objective Optimization Problems (MOP), where
multiple conﬂicting objectives must be optimized simultaneously.
Pertinent to this class of
problems is that no unique solution exists. Instead, a set of Pareto optimal solutions that are
mathematically equally good can be identiﬁed through mathematical modelling [3]. Similarly,
as the size of the decision-making problem increases, it becomes increasingly diﬃcult for the
decision-maker to choose a preferred solution, and the problem becomes more computationally
complex.
Traditionally, the MCDM and MOP communities have evolved separately. However, a growing
interest in the synergies between these two ﬁelds has emerged in recent years. This integration
promises to leverage the combined strengths of both ﬁelds while addressing their weaknesses [4].
Such synergy is particularly valuable when addressing real-life optimization problems. However,
diﬃculties remain, such as eﬀectively handling many objectives, including the preferences of
the decision-makers (DMs) in the decision-making process and computational complexity [5].
Therefore, many state-of-the-art hybrid approaches utilize evolutionary algorithms in combina-
==PAGE:14==
2
1.1. THE STRUCTURE OF THE MASTER’S THESIS
tion with MCDM methods. However, approaches that use MCDM methods to ease the search
process of exact methods have yet to be seen.
The purpose of this master’s thesis is to investigate the interface between classical exact MOP
methods and widely used MCDM methods to seek an answer to the following question: How can
a step-by-step decision-making framework combining MCDM and MOP methods be designed to
address real-life optimization problems involving multiple objectives and multiple stakeholders,
while addressing the shortcomings of existing approaches? To answer this question, the following
objectives are considered: (a) Scalability, (b) Involvement of multiple stakeholders, and (c)
Reducing the burden of decision-makers.
The proposed framework will be tested and validated on a simulation of the signiﬁcant, real-life
combinatorial multi-objective optimization problem of distributing high school students in the
Capital Region of Denmark.
1.1
The structure of the master’s thesis
Throughout the master’s thesis, the focus will be on addressing the main question. The second
chapter will brieﬂy overview the general ideas of MCDM and MOP. The third chapter sum-
marises a literature review of existing MCDM and MOP methods. In the fourth chapter, areas
of concern based on the literature review are outlined, and the choice of methodologies is dis-
cussed. The proposed framework and the hybridization of the selected methods are presented
in the subsequent section. Next, the proposed framework is applied to the target problem and
presented according to the steps outlined in the framework. The seventh chapter presents the
results obtained from the case study. In the eighth chapter, a discussion is carried out based on
the results obtained and the proposed framework, along with a sensitivity analysis. Finally, the
conclusion is presented, encompassing future work concerning the proposed framework.
==PAGE:15==
Chapter 2
Background
The following chapter contains information to help understand the basic concepts of the thesis.
It is especially relevant for readers without prior knowledge of Multi-Criteria Decision Analysis
and multi-objective optimization.
2.1
Multi-Criteria Decision Analysis
Often real-life problems include conﬂicting criteria or goals that must be considered simulta-
neously. Such problems are known as multiple-criterion decision-making (MCDM) problems
[3]. In most cases, the decision-makers (DMs) seek to choose the optimal solution. However, a
genuinely optimal solution is only attainable when a single objective or goal is considered [6].
Multi-Criteria Decision Analysis (MCDA) or MCDM can be perceived as a tool to aid DMs
in guiding decision-making in producing a satisfactory output given a set of ﬁnite alternatives
according to multiple criteria. Emphasizing the initial term, the methodology is designed to ’aid’
or assist the decision maker in deliberation. Moreover, MCDA can encompass both quantitative
and qualitative indicators on the criteria, thus allowing subjective priorities and perspectives of
the DMs to be considered [6]. There exist numerous methods within MCDA, but they often
share the following denominators [1]:
• Identify meaningful criteria and alternatives.
• Assign numerical values to each criterion based on their signiﬁcance and evaluate the
performance of each alternative concerning these criteria.
• Process the numerical values to ﬁnd the ranking of the alternatives.
The MCDA technique fosters a participatory approach by transferring the decision-making from
the analyst to the stakeholders involved. Utilizing MCDA as a decision-making tool can enhance
satisfaction for the decision-makers, both in the decision-making process and the outcome [6].
2.2
Multi-objective optimization problems
Many real-life optimization problems often involve multiple, sometimes opposing, objectives that
must be considered simultaneously. This pool of problems can be regarded as Multi-objective
Optimization problems (MOPs). However, they can be mathematically modelled and solved
through multi-objective optimization [7]. A deterministic MOP can be described as [7]:
minimize
{f1(x), . . . , fk(x)}
subject to
x ∈S
(2.1)
==PAGE:16==
4
2.2. MULTI-OBJECTIVE OPTIMIZATION PROBLEMS
including conﬂicting objective functions fi : Rn →R to be simultaneously optimized, where
1 ≤i ≤k and k ≥2. The objective vectors f(x) = (f1(x), . . . , fk(x))T are composed of objective
function values determined by the decision vectors x = (x1, x2, . . . , xn)T . The decision vectors
belong to the nonempty feasible set S ⊂Rn [3]. Multi-objective optimization addresses two
equally important tasks: the optimization task of identifying Pareto optimal solutions and the
decision-making task of selecting a single most preferred solution.
The latter often requires
preference information from a DM [3].
Pareto optimal solutions refer to the characteristic where no unique solution exists; instead, a
set of mathematically equally good solutions can be identiﬁed. An objective function can be
considered Pareto optimal if it is impossible to improve any of its components without causing
deterioration in at least one other component. To elaborate, the decision vector x′ is called
Pareto optimal if there does not exist another x ∈S such that fi(x) ≤fi(x′) for all i = 1, . . . , k
and fj(x) < fj(x′) for at least one index j [3]. The set of Pareto optimal solutions is contained
within the set of weakly Pareto optimal solutions. A decision vector x′ is weakly Pareto optimal
if no other solution exists in x ∈S such that fi(x) < fi(x′) for all i = 1, · · · , k. [3].
==PAGE:17==
Chapter 3
Literature Review
To begin with, existing methods within the literature of MCDM are outlined in section 3.1. The
section is predominately based on the compendium by Barfod and Lelueur (2022) [6] for the
course Decision Support and Strategic Assessment. Additional literature has been uncovered
using DTU Findit, Google Scholar and ScienceDirect, covering publications between 1976 and
2023.
Next, existing methods within the literature of MOP are outlined in section 3.2. The section is
mainly based on the books by Miettinen et al. (2008) [3] and Miettinen (1998) [8]. Additional
articles and literature have been identiﬁed using DTU Findit, Google Scholar and ScienceDirect,
covering publications between 1971 and 2021.
Finally, literature on the combination of MCDM and MOP methods is outlined in section 3.3.
The articles have been found through DTU Findit, Google Scholar and ScienceDirect, covering
publications between 2010 and 2023.
3.1
Methods for Multi-Criteria Decision-Making
One of the most well-known branches of methods within decision-making is MCDM [1]. The
research ﬁeld has evolved signiﬁcantly over the past decades in tandem with new application
areas, as new methods are developed and old ones are improved[2]. The dogma is that the
methods within MCDM can be divided into two distinct ﬁelds, the American school and the
French school [9]. The works of Keeney and Raiﬀa spearhead the former [10]. The latter was
founded by B. Roy (as cited in, [9]).
3.1.1
The American School
The common denominator for methods within the American school is an aggregation-based ap-
proach to arrive at an overall score for each alternative given a set of criteria and weights. The
assumption that the DM’s preferences are complete is pertinent to the American school, allowing
the DMs to articulate their preferences comprehensively across all dimensions of the decision
problem [6]. One of the foundational methods within this ﬁeld is the Multi-Attribute Value The-
ory (MAVT). A derivative of MAVT, where uncertainty is accounted for, is the Multi-Attribute
Utility Theory (MAUT) [6]. Another widely applied method is the Simple Multi-Attribute Rat-
ing Technique (SMART), one of the simplest forms of MAUT. However, it does not explicitly
account for uncertainty and risk as MAUT does [2]. An alternative widely utilized method,
when multiple objectives are considered, is the Analytic Hierarchy Process (AHP) [6]. AHP
diﬀerentiates itself from the previously mentioned methods, employing pairwise comparisons to
elicit weights and determine the performance of a set of alternatives [2].
==PAGE:18==
6
3.2. METHODS IN MULTI-OBJECTIVE OPTIMIZATION
3.1.2
The French School
The common denominator for methods within the French school is the construction of outrank-
ing relations based on a set of criteria. Pertinent to the French school is the questioning of
the existence of a complete and well-structured preference system [6]. A pioneering collection of
methods within this ﬁeld is the ELECTRE (ELimination Et Choix Traduisant la REalité) family
founded by B. Roy (as cited in, [9]. The ELECTRE methods utilize binary outranking relations
to establish an outranking relation on a set of alternatives instead of employing an underpinning
aggregative value function [6]. The various iterations of ELECTRE, such as ELECTRE I, II, III,
and ELECTRE-TRI, diﬀer in complexity, richness and applicability to diﬀerent decision prob-
lems. PROMETHEE (Preference Ranking Organization Method for Enrichment Evaluation)
is another prominent method within the French school [11].
PROMETHEE utilizes prefer-
ence functions to determine the outranking relation on a set of alternatives. Like ELECTRE,
PROMETHEE has various iterations, such as PROMETHEE I, II, and PROMSORT.
3.1.3
Advantages and disadvantages of MCDM methods
Selecting an approach and method(s) entirely depends on the problem, area of interest, desired
outcome, and available resources. The MCDA approach oﬀers a participatory decision-making
process for the DMs involved [6]. Moreover, it delimits a broad spectrum of perspectives able
to organize and structure the decision-making process, often leading to a more balanced and
comprehensive assessment of the possibilities at hand [4].
Some MCDM methods allow the
DMs to account for monetary and non-monetary criteria by incorporating subjective weights.
However, due to the nature of MCDA methods, decision-making arguments advocate for a
particular viewpoint rather than being based on an entirely objective analysis [4]. The inclusive
element of MCDM methods can make decision-making time-consuming and resource-intensive.
This is further emphasized by the fact that it is rare for only one DM to be involved in a real-life
decision-making process. Furthermore, as the number of criteria grows, it poses a signiﬁcant
challenge to the cognitive stamina required by the DMs in the decision-making process [12].
Another challenge is the process of eliciting weights, scores and other necessary parameters,
as these concepts can be challenging to explain in layman’s terms [2]. Despite the challenges,
employing MCDA method(s) in a decision-making process can enhance the decision-making
process and the quality of the decision itself [6].
3.2
Methods in Multi-objective Optimization
Multi-objective optimization problems are a distinct class of problems where multiple, often
conﬂicting, objectives must be optimized simultaneously. One characteristic is that no unique
solutions exist, but a set of equally good solutions can be found. These solutions are referred
to as non-dominated, non-inferior, trade-oﬀor Pareto optimal solutions [3]. In the literature
of MOPs, these terms are used interchangeably. Many of the ideas and methods used to solve
this class of problems by optimization are built upon theory from mathematical programming.
Extensive research within MOPs has been conducted and still is, let alone the 500+ papers
produced between 1955 and 1986 [3].
Due to the yield and sharing of competent literature
on the matter, this chapter is restricted to some of the most well-known methodologies. The
common denominator in the literature is that solving MOPs is generally viewed as aiding a
human DM in simultaneously considering multiple objectives and identifying a Pareto optimal
solution that best satisﬁes their preferences. Besides including a DM, solving MOPs necessitates
an analyst or expert driving the decision-making process’s mathematical modelling or computing
aspect [3]. By considering the role of the DM in the decision-making process, four classes of
approaches can be derived [8]:
• No preference methods
==PAGE:19==
3.2. METHODS IN MULTI-OBJECTIVE OPTIMIZATION
7
• A priori methods
• A posteriori methods
• Interactive methods
A brief overview of two basic yet frequently applied methods is presented before covering well-
known methods within the classes above.
3.2.1
Classical Methods
The two Classical methods, the weighting method and ϵ-constraint method, are utilized to
convert the problem into a state, where one objective function can be optimized by single
objective solvers [3]. The distinction between the modelling and optimization phases is often
blurry.
Weighting Method
In the weighting method, the following problem is solved [3].

as weakly Pareto optimal. However, if the solution is unique or if wi > 0 for all i = 1, · · · , k it
is Pareto optimal [8]. The weighting method is versatile in that it can be employed in a priori,
where the DM speciﬁes weights before the optimization, and a posteriori, where weights are
used to generate diﬀerent solutions, and the DM is tasked to pick the most preferred one [3].
ε-constraint method
In the ε-constraint method, one of the objective functions is chosen to be optimized, whereas
the rest are transformed into constraints, which yields the following form of the problem [3]:
minimize

optimal if it is a unique solution of (3.2) for some l with εj = fj(x∗) for j = 1, . . . , k, j ̸= l [3].
3.2.2
No Preference Methods
As for the class of no preference methods, the DM’s preferences are not accounted for in the
solution process. More precisely, no explicit preferences from the DM are available, and thus,
they cannot be utilized [3]. Consequently, a relatively simple method is often employed to solve
the MOP to arrive at a compromise solution, which is then presented to the DM. No preference
methods apply when the DM has no expectation of the outcome or is simply satisﬁed with an
arbitrary optimal solution [8]. Nevertheless, this class of methods can also be utilized to initiate
interactive methods. The global criterion method can be used as a representative of this class.
Global Criterion
In the global criterion method [13], the distance between the feasible objective region and a
desirable reference point is sought to be minimized. The analysts select the reference point,
often choosing it as the ideal objective vector [3]. In this case, all objectives are considered to

8

as proven in [8].
3.2.3
A priori methods
The characteristic of the a priori methods is that they necessitate that the DM(s) express their
preferences before the solution process [3]. In this section, two approaches are outlined.
Value function method
An eﬀective way to ﬁnd Pareto optimal solutions is the value function method [10], given that
the DM knows an explicit mathematical formula for the value function that accurately captures
and represents all their preferences [3]. In such instances, the problem to be solved is:
maximize
v(f(x))
subject to
x ∈S,
(3.5)
The value function yields a complete ordering of the objective space, identifying the best Pareto
optimal solution.
Goal programming
The general idea of goal programming is that the DM speciﬁes aspiration levels, often optimistic,
for the selected objective functions, and any deviations from these aspirations are sought to be
minimized. A goal is comprised of an objective function and an aspiration level. Objective
functions are denoted fi, and the belonging aspiration levels as ¯zi for i = 1, . . . , k. For min-
imization problems, goals are formulated as fi(x) ≤¯zi [8]. To initiate the solution process,
the decision-makers are required to specify the aspiration levels for each of the chosen objective
functions.
The following tasks minimize the downward and upward deviations of the objective function
values aﬃliated with each aspiration level ¯zi. That is δi = ¯zi −fi(x). The deviation variable
is denoted as δi, which can be positive and negative contingent upon the problem. It can be
represented as the diﬀerence between two positive variables as δi = δ−
i −δ+
i , where δ−
i
is a
downward deviation, and δ+
i is an upward deviation about the aspiration level.
In the domain of goal programming, two primary approaches are utilized. One is a weighted (also
known as Archimedean) approach, and the other is a lexicographic (also known as preemptive)
approach. In the weighted approach, the weighted sum of the deviational variables is minimized.
In practice, besides specifying aspiration levels, the DM must determine the importance of
reaching said aspiration levels using weighting coeﬃcients. This can mathematically be expressed

In the lexicographic approach, besides stating the aspiration levels, the DM must determine a
lexicographic order for the speciﬁed goals according to their absolute importance [8]. The highest
priority goal must signiﬁcantly surpass the second priority goal in importance. Subsequently, the
problem (3.6) is addressed sequentially as follows: First, the deviational variables for the most
crucial goal are minimized subject to the original constraints. If a unique solution is discovered,
it represents the solution to the complete multi-objective optimization problem [8]. If not, the
second most crucial goal is minimized. However, along with the original constraints, a new
constraint is added to preserve the optimal value of the most important goal. If this problem
yields a unique solution, it is the solution to the original multi-objective optimization problem.
Else, the process is reiterated [8].
3.2.4
A posteriori methods
A ﬁtting synonym for a posteriori methods could be methods for generating Pareto optimal
solutions. This class’s characteristic is that the DMs’ preference information is revealed after
the Pareto front is found [3]. The notion is that the DM is provided with an overview of the
respective optimal solutions, making the selection of the most preferred trade-oﬀsolution easier
for the DM [14]. A posteriori methods are most suitable for MOPs with 2 or 3 objectives, where
it is possible to illustrate the trade-oﬀsolutions directly [14].
The weighted metrics method is one standard method for generating a Pareto optimal solution
in this context. This method extends the idea of the global criterion method, see chapter 3.2.2,
by assigning a set of weighting coeﬃcients to each objective function [3]. Diﬀerent solutions can
be computed and presented to the DM by alternating the weighting coeﬃcients associated with
the objective functions. To ensure Pareto optimality, it can be proven if all weights are positive
or if a unique solution exists [8].
In addition to the weighted metrics method and other classical a posteriori methods, the Tcheby-
cheﬀmathematical program with Partition of the objective space using the Alternating of di-
rections (TPA) algorithm represents a signiﬁcant advancement in generating Pareto optimal
solutions. Developed by Domínguez-Ríos et al. (2021) [15], TPA is classiﬁed as an anytime
algorithm, meaning it can be terminated at any moment during its execution and still return
a well-spread set of optimal solutions. The algorithm leverages an existing method to solve
Multi-objective combinatorial optimization (MOCO) problems combined with partitioning the
objective space using alternating directions [15]. This allows the TPA algorithm to solve any
MOCO problem eﬀectively and can be applied to multi-objective discrete optimization (MODO)
problems with ﬁnite feasible sets.
An extensive experimental study compared it with other
state-of-the-art anytime algorithms, revealing that the TPA algorithm outperforms the diﬀerent
algorithms in most instances [15].
==PAGE:22==
10
3.2. METHODS IN MULTI-OBJECTIVE OPTIMIZATION
3.2.5
Interactive methods
In the following section, some of the basic ideas and properties of interactive methods will
be outlined. In the context of MOP, interactive methods seek to identify the most preferred
solutions of the DM through an iterative search process, where the DM provides preferences
progressively doing the solution process [16]. To rephrase, the preference elicitation and solution
process alternates until the DM is satisﬁed with a single solution [3]. After each iteration, some
information is provided to the DMs, and they are requested to evaluate the proposed solutions or
provide additional details capturing their current preferences. The participation and interest of
the DM in the iterative solution process is therefore vital to the interactive methods [3]. As for
the more classical interactive methods, they vary in terms of how information is both captured
and given and how the ﬁnal solution is determined, including the nature of the solution (e.g.,
Pareto optimal) and the problem assumptions and convergence properties [3].
Regarding how information is speciﬁed, classic interactive methods can be categorized into
three types: classiﬁcation-based methods, trade-oﬀinformation methods, and reference point
approaches.
The idea behind classiﬁcation-based methods is that the DM indicates preferences by classifying
the objective functions. These classes reﬂect what kind of changes in the objective values would
lead to a more preferred solution. Opposed to the trade-oﬀ-based methods and their dependence
on mathematical convergence [3], the basic philosophy of classiﬁcation-based methods is that the
search process stops when the decision maker (DM) decides that a set of satisfactory preferred
solutions has been found.
This idea is shared by the reference point-based methods. However, the diﬀerence is that while
classiﬁcation-based methods assume that some objective value must worsen for others to improve,
a reference point can be chosen freely.
The search process of classiﬁcation-based methods is more transparent and allows the DM to
be in control by selecting functions to be improved, specifying amounts of relaxation for others,
and expressing preference information as reference points if desired [3]. At this moment, the
DM can learn about the problem and the interrelationship between the objectives and adjust
expectations correspondingly.
For the classiﬁcation-based methods, NIMBUS [8], Synchronous NIMBUS method [17], STEM
[18] and STOM [19] are signiﬁcant. For trade-oﬀmethods, the Geoﬀrion, Dyer and Feinberg
(GDF) algorithm can be highlighted [20]. For the reference point approach, the reference point
method can be mentioned[21]. Other methods exist besides the listed categories of interactive
methods, such as MOEAs with interactive elements.
However, none can be claimed to be
superior to the other as it entirely depends on the circumstances[3].
3.2.6
Evolutionary optimization Approach
Multi-Objective Optimization Evolutionary Algorithms (MOEAs) are population-based stochas-
tic search procedures capable of identifying a representative subset of the Pareto front in a single
simulation. MOEAs have garnered a lot of attention in the research ﬁeld of computational in-
telligence due to their broad application and suitability in relation to MOPs [3]. MOEAs are
based upon evolutionary algorithms (EAs), which are greatly inspired by survival-of-the-ﬁttest
evolutionary principles found in nature. They apply genetically motivated recombination and
mutation of solutions to iteratively create a new and hopefully better population of solutions
until a pre-deﬁned termination criterion is met [3].
The general procedure of the evolutionary algorithms varies depending on the speciﬁc method.
However, the basic principles are the same. The algorithms start with some initial population
==PAGE:23==
3.2. METHODS IN MULTI-OBJECTIVE OPTIMIZATION
11
P0. How this population is created depends on the speciﬁc problem and method used. However,
a random feasible solution is usually created [3]. Then, the algorithm performs a set of iterative
operations, updating the current population to create a new one. The main operators are
• Selection - This operation selects the better members of the population.
• Crossover - This operation chooses two or more solutions (parents) to create one or more
new solutions (children).
• Mutation - This operation applies random changes to a single solution.
• Elitist - This operation maintains the ’best’ of the parent and child solutions.
In the context of MOPs, the notable distinction between an EA approach and a MOEA approach
is that the EAs traditionally focus on a single objective. In contrast, MOEAs focus on multiple
often conﬂicting objectives simultaneously [3]. Examples of popular employed MOEAs are the
elitist Non-dominated Sorting Genetic Algorithm II (NSGA-II) [22] and the Strength Pareto
Evolutionary Algorithm 2 (SPEA2) [23]. Each MOEA aims to achieve a well-diversiﬁed approx-
imation of the Pareto front. NSGA-II employs an elitist principle, explicit diversity preserving
mechanism and focuses on non-dominated solutions [22]. SPEA2 utilizes an external archived
set, ﬁtness test, and a clustering algorithm to achieve and maintain convergence and diversity
in the solution set [23].
3.2.7
Estimation of the nadir point
Estimating the nadir point is central to previously outlined methods, such as the classical in-
teractive optimization techniques in section 3.2.5. The nadir point represents the worst Pareto
optimal solution of each objective [3]. Along with the ideal point (a point constructed by the
best value of each objective, which can be determined by optimizing each objective function in-
dividually), the nadir point is used to normalize the objective functions which are often desired
for the adequate functioning of a MOP algorithm [24]. Knowing the ideal and nadir objective
values helps the DMs adjust their realistic expectations by knowing each objective’s range.
Since the nadir point represents the worst values of each objective, estimating the point ne-
cessitates information about the entire Pareto front. Therefore, the methods employed should
involve ﬁnding Pareto-optimal solutions. However, approximating intermediate Pareto-optimal
solutions is not needed to estimate the nadir point since only the extreme objective values of the
Pareto front are of interest. Doing so eﬀectively has proven challenging, and diﬀerent approaches
have been suggested to overcome this diﬃculty [24].
Benayoun et al. (1971) [18] suggested using payoﬀtables to estimate the nadir point. In this
method, assuming minimization, a table is constructed where the ith row represents values of
all other objective functions calculated at the point where the ith objective is minimum. The
nadir point is then approximated by identifying the highest (worst-case) values for each objective
across the table, representing the upper bounds of the Pareto-optimal set.
Methods within MOEAs have been gaining popularity recently because of their capability to
discover multiple, well-diversiﬁed approximations of Pareto-optimal solutions simultaneously.
They can be an ideal way to ﬁnd the nadir objective vector [24]. In the paper by Deb et al.
(2014) [24], diﬀerent modiﬁcations to the NSGA-II procedure are proposed to eﬃciently and
reliably approximate the nadir point. The extremized crowded NSGA-II performed the best
over a series of tests with problems ranging from 2-20 objectives compared to unaltered NSGA-
II [24]. Unlike NSGA-II, the proposed procedure’s crowding distance is modiﬁed to emphasize
the extreme points rather than maintaining a diverse population [24]. Noteworthy, it is suggested
==PAGE:24==
12
3.2. METHODS IN MULTI-OBJECTIVE OPTIMIZATION
that the proposed MOEA poses no signiﬁcant advantage over NSGA-II or an equivalent approach
for three objectives or less.
3.2.8
Advantages and disadvantages of MOP methods
The selection of a method for solving an MOP entirely depends on the decision problem, the
DM(s) aspirations, and the resources available. Each class of approach has its advantages and
disadvantages, as well as the belonging methods described.
The classical methods proposed in section 3.2.1 can be applied in both a priori and a posteriori.
However, they have some severe shortcomings. The weighting method is supposed to be applied
to convex problems; however, it is not always straightforward to determine the convexity in
real-life applications. If used in relation to non-convex problems, the DM will be presented with
a severe misrepresentation of the Pareto optimal set as only locally Pareto optimal solutions
might be found [3]. The ϵ-constraint method can be applied to convex and non-convex problems.
Even so, specifying the bounds can be a challenging and rigid task of representing DM preference
information [3], and computationally, the ϵ-constraint method is more laborious compared to
the weighting method, as the number of constraints increases [8].
As for the class of no preference mentioned in section 3.2.2, a method such as the global criterion
method can be employed when no preference information from a DM is available or as a ﬁrst
step in an interactive approach, yet it seems highly unlikely, that using such a method would be
able to identify a solution that best satisﬁes a DM [3].
As described in section 3.2.3, an a priori approach, such as the value function method, can be
highly eﬀective if the preferences of DM can truly be captured in a mathematical model [14].
However, in practice, this can be a very challenging task and, at times, impossible [3]. Regarding
practical application, goal programming is recognized as one of the most widely used methods
for MOP, partly due to its longevity. It is also well understood by DMs in decision-making [8].
However, determining adequate aspiration levels to ensure Pareto optimality can be challenging,
especially if the DM is unaware of the feasible region. Regarding the lexicographic approach,
specifying an order for the objectives by utmost importance also poses a challenge. Moreover, if
the most important goal has a unique solution, no impact is registered from the remaining goals
in the decision-making approach [8]. Employing an a priori approach can be eﬀective, but the
DM must have extensive knowledge about the decision problem before the solution process.
As outlined in section 3.2.4, a posteriori methods are adept at generating Pareto optimal solu-
tions, allowing the DM to get an overview and select a preferred solution[16]. However, common
among the methods employed in a state of a posteriori is a potentially high computational bur-
den, in particular, when the number of objectives increases, as the number of solutions required
to describe the Pareto front gets very large [14], [16]. Furthermore, the cognitive burden for the
DMs to select a single solution from many solutions can be a challenge in itself [3], [14].
As detailed in section 3.2.5, interactive methods employ an iterative search process, where the
DM gradually provides preference information, subsequently guiding the solution process. This
constructive process enables the DM to learn about the depth of the decision problem, oﬀsetting
the need for a global or predeﬁned preference structure as with a posteriori and a priori methods
[3]. As only regions of interest are explored, only parts of the Pareto front have to be generated,
thus making it less computationally taxing than a posteriori methods and more accessible to
choose between solutions [3], [16]. The main drawback of employing interactive methods is that
it necessitates intense involvement from the DMs [16]. Moreover, many interactive methods
focus little on a support system that enables the DM to make a ﬁnal choice of the subset of
preferred solutions.
==PAGE:25==
3.3. HYBRID MCDA AND MOP METHODS
13
MOEAs, as presented in section 3.2.6, oﬀer an eﬃcient way to ﬁnd a representative subset of
the Pareto front for MOPs in a single run [5]. An algorithm, such as NSGA-II, performs well
for non-convex MOPs with two objectives [22]. However, NSGA-II and SPEA2, which rely on
comparing solutions based on Pareto dominance, struggle with problems with many objectives.
This is because as objectives increase, most solutions are equally good. This makes it diﬃcult
for the algorithm to distinguish between the solutions and pick the best ones, noteworthy when
there is only a limited number of solutions in the population [25], [26].
Estimating the nadir point, described in section 3.2.7, is necessary for some interactive methods.
A way to estimate the nadir point is by using payoﬀtables. However, the main diﬃculty of the
payoﬀtable method is that an objective’s worst-case value may not occur at the points where
other objectives achieve their best values. This can lead to inaccurate nadir point estimation,
as the highest values in the payoﬀtable may not truly represent the worst-case scenarios for
each objective, resulting in potential underestimation of the nadir point [24].
Iserman and
Steuer (1988) [27] highlighted the diﬃculty of ﬁnding a nadir point, emphasizing the need
to use methods better than the payoﬀtable method. An alternative approach would be using
MOEAs, such as NSGA-II or SPEA2. This is logical because estimating the nadir point requires
approximating the entire Pareto front. Yet, intermediate Pareto optimal solutions are not of
interest for this task. Therefore, methods emphasizing the extreme solutions in relation to the
objective functions are preferred, such as the extremized crowded NSGA-II. Nevertheless, due
to the inherent nature of estimating the nadir point, it remains a problematic task [24].
3.3
Hybrid MCDA and MOP Methods
For many years, methods within the traditional MCDM community have predominantly devel-
oped in isolation from those within the MOP community. Yet, in recent years, there has been
a growing recognition of the synergies between these ﬁelds, leading to new waves of research
combining their strengths and mitigating individual weaknesses [4]. Both ﬁelds are concerned
with ﬁnding satisfactory solutions for the DMs.
Within MOP, MOEA excels at approximating Pareto optimal solutions; however, it cannot often
assist DMs in choosing preferred solutions from a potentially enormous set of well-diversiﬁed
collection of trade-oﬀoptimal solutions [28]. Conversely, methods within MCDM are powerful at
eliciting preferences and incorporating them in the decision-making process but cannot handle
many alternatives and criteria simultaneously. By incorporating MCDM procedures into MOP
methods, especially when solving MOPs with more objectives than three, often referred to
as Many-Objective Optimization problems (MaOPs), researchers aim to mitigate some of the
challenges the DMs might encounter.
Beyond the mentioned challenges, additional diﬃculties arise when providing precise preferences
for diﬀerent objectives and comprehending conﬂicts and correlations between the respective
objectives[5]. Nevertheless, many MCDM-MOP hybrid methods still experience diﬃculties when
faced with many objectives, often reﬂected in real-life problems in computational complexity and
preference modelling [28],[14].
As with methods within MOP, three classiﬁcations of hybrid methods can be made. A priori, a
posteriori and interactive methods [14].
After reviewing much of the most promising literature surrounding hybrid methods, it was found
that no single combination of MCDA and MOP methods can encompass every real-life problem.
However, many promising approaches and combinations have presented themselves for both
MOPs and MaOPs.
==PAGE:26==
14
3.4. PERSONAS - A DESIGN THINKING METHOD
• The DM’s preference proﬁles are elicited at the beginning and used to guide the search
of the MOP method towards regions of interest for the DM [4]. In the following article
by Karahan and Köksalan (2010), a mechanism was developed to incorporate preference
information into the MOEA to explore desired regions as speciﬁed by the DM [29].
• The use of an MOEA to generate an approximation of the Pareto front, followed by an
MCDM method to aid the DM in selecting the most preferred solution from the approxi-
mated set [4]. In a paper by Malekmohammadi et al. (2011), ELECTRE TRI was utilized
to rank solutions found by an MOEA, speciﬁcally NSGA-II, for optimizing reservoir oper-
ations [30].
• Integrating MOEA and MCDM methods in an iterative manner. Initially, the MOEA runs
for a few generations, after which MCDA methods capture user preferences. Subsequently,
the captured preference information is used to guide the following MOEA’s generation,
and the process is repeated until the DM is satisﬁed [4]. An example of this can be found
in the study by Kesireddy et al. (2020), where an MCDM method is utilized to pick the
best MOEA policies to exploit the solution space [31] eﬀectively.
• Another possible framework sought to mitigate the challenges of a priori, a posteriori and
interactive is to introduce each approach step-wise in conjunction with an MOEA, as seen
in [5].
In the literature, the most recent hybrid MCDM-MOP methods build upon MOEA and MCDM
procedures to solve MaOPs. However, alternative approaches have been explored, such as uti-
lizing machine learning-based approaches interlinked with MCDM procedures to navigate both
the objective and decision space [32]. Traditionally, the ﬁeld of MOP, particularly MOEAs,
has been concerned with identifying a single solution from an approximated Pareto front, often
neglecting or simplifying preference information provided by DMs. Similarly, MCDM methods
have focused on selecting or ranking alternatives from an already-known solution space.
With today’s increased computational capabilities and the increasing complexity of data, there
is a need for hybrid methods that can address real-life MaOPs. This trend is driving a promising
and growing research ﬁeld that integrates the strengths and capabilities of both MCDM and
MOP to enhance the decision-making process [33].
3.4
Personas - a Design Thinking Method
Design thinking’s theoretical foundation stems from various disciplines such as engineering, de-
sign, social sciences, and the like, where people were working on devising improved or alternative
ways to solve complex or ill-deﬁned problems [34]. In general, the belief is that not all issues are
adequately solved using an economic or rational approach, but instead leveraging design think-
ing or human-centred design as a viable approach to dealing with complex or real-life problems
[34]. Opposite to rational problem-solving focused on critical thinking, design thinking employs
creativity and critical-thinking aspects when addressing a problem [34]. One commonly used
method within design thinking is the creation of personas [35]. Initially utilized in IT system
development, the method has since expanded into various other ﬁelds, such as product devel-
opment, communications planning, marketing, and service design. Although developed in the
1990s, the methodology is not rigid and can be modulated in diﬀerent ways to suit the speciﬁc
circumstance in which it is applied[36]. The persona methodology encompasses precise descrip-
tions of ﬁctional characters or user characteristics based on sound ﬁeld work[37].
Engaging
persona descriptions often include the following [36]:
• Appearance: Provide context to the persona’s appearance through text or image.
==PAGE:27==
3.4. PERSONAS - A DESIGN THINKING METHOD
15
• Social background: Social factors inﬂuencing actions such as education, culture or organi-
zation.
• Psyche: Underlying motivators.
• Relation to focus area: Connection to the system or product at hand.
The gist of employing personas is to provide insights into the demographics, behaviours, desires,
goals and needs of the targeted segment emphatically, thereby aiding decision-making related
to a product, service or system[36].
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 4
Chosen Methods
The general concepts, advantages, and limitations of methods within MCDM, MOP, and the
hybridization of the two research ﬁelds have been presented. These ﬁndings will now be contex-
tualized concerning multi-objective optimization problems involving many stakeholders. This
analysis has highlighted several key areas of concern.
• (a) Scalability.
• (b) Involvement of multiple stakeholders.
• (c) Reducing the burden of decision-makers.
• (d) Performance assessment of hybridized MCDM and MOP methods.
A common problem shared amongst many of the described methods is their applicability to
problems encompassing many objectives, a pertinent characteristic of many real-world problems.
Beyond the computational complexity, this trait extends into the preference elicitation process
and the accuracy of the preference proﬁles of the DM, as it can be cumbersome for the DM
to comprehend many objectives simultaneously [14].
Moreover, as the number of objectives
increases, so does the number of Pareto optimal solutions constituting the Pareto front, making
the selection of one solution hard.
Numerous MaOPs involve decisions that must consider the interest of multiple stakeholders [3].
These decisions are often encountered in public policy. Yet, most MCDM, MOP, and hybrid
derivatives assume that only a single DM is involved in the solution process. Even when the
ﬁnal decision is made by a single individual, such as a politician, that individual must consider
the interests of diﬀerent parties. Therefore, appropriate measures should be explored to deal
with divided preferences.
The cumbersome tasks involved in the decision-making process for MaOPs, especially with
interactive methods, can impose high burdens on the DMs.
Xin et al.
(2018) [16] classify
two types of burdens for interactive methods: the cognitive burden of providing preferences
and the number of interactions between the DM and the algorithm. Excessive touch points
between the algorithm and the DM might induce fatigue, making the DM more prone to mistakes
and requiring extensive resources.
Determining when the solution process should end is no
trivial task, particularly for interactive methods. These burdens can result in errors, or the
solution process is prematurely terminated [16]. Consequently, reducing the DM’s burdens is an
important area and reducing the resources needed to carry out the solution process.
==PAGE:29==
4.1. SYNCHRONOUS NIMBUS METHOD
17
Despite a moderate body of literature on hybridized MCDM and MOP methods and interactive
methods, there is an inherent gap in assessments that can provide performance measures on how
well an approach can satisfy the needs of the DM [14], [16].
Two of the respective methods will be explored in greater detail to understand the choice of
methods to deal with the aforementioned areas of concern.
4.1
Synchronous NIMBUS Method
In this section, the synchronous NIMBUS method, presented in [17], will be described in detail,
along with a short presentation of the four scalarizing functions used in the method.
The synchronous NIMBUS method is based on classifying objective functions into ﬁve classes
that reﬂect the desires of the DM at a current solution. It was initially developed to deal with
demanding non-linear multi-objective optimization problems and utilizes scalarizing functions
to transform the multi-objective problem into a single-objective problem that can be solved with
conventional methods [17]. The main idea is to use several scalarizing functions since they may
lead to entirely diﬀerent Pareto optimal solutions even though they reﬂect the same preference
information [38].
This approach increases the method’s versatility and variety of solutions
without altering the preference information artiﬁcially.
These diﬀerent scalarizing functions
may treat the information in a strict or more indicative way, allowing the DM to compare the
outcomes and judge which solution is most satisfactory.
The following concepts assume minimization without loss of generality.
• x = (x1, x2, . . . , xn)T belongs to the non-empty compact feasible region S ⊂Rn
• f(x) = (f1 (x) , f2 (x) , . . . , fn (x))T .
• f (S) is the feasible objective region.
• z⋆is the ideal objective vector containing the objective values of minimizing each objective
function individually subject to the feasible region S.
• z⋆⋆is the utopian objective vector. This vector is strictly better than z⋆. In practice, it is
set to be z⋆⋆
i
= z⋆
i −ϵ for i = 1, . . . , k for some small ϵ > 0.
• znad is the nadir objective vector. This is the upper bound of the Pareto optimal solution
set. It is generally diﬃcult to obtain the exact vector in cases with more than two objectives
(see section 3.2.7 for more information on the subject).
• z is an aspiration-level vector containing reference points of the DM. It is assumed that
zi ≥z⋆
i for i = 1, . . . , k, meaning that the DM does not have expectations beyond the ideal
values.
The classiﬁcation process of the synchronous NIMBUS method starts with presenting objective
function values fi(xc) for i = 1, . . . , k calculated at a current Pareto optimal decision vector
xc ∈S to the DM. The DM states if the solution is satisfactory. If not, the DM classiﬁes the
objective functions i = 1, . . . , k into the following ﬁve classes [17]:
• I< whose values are desired to be improved from the current level.
• I⩽whose values are desired to be improved to some aspiration level ˆzi < fi (xc)
• I= whose values are accepted as is.
• I⩾whose values may be impaired till some upper bound εi > fi (xc).
==PAGE:30==
18

• I⋄whose values are temporarily allowed to change freely.
By the deﬁnition of Pareto optimality, a classiﬁcation is feasible only if at least one objective
function is allowed to be worse for another to improve.

When comparing the subproblems (4.1)-(4.4), the most signiﬁcant diﬀerence is that the classiﬁ-
cations are strictly obeyed in (4.1) and are based on the current solution. In contrast, the other
==PAGE:31==
4.2. ELECTRE
19
subproblems treat the preference information more suggestively with no strict bounds and can
be formed independently of the current solution. Further, in terms of the optimality of the four
subproblems, they are guaranteed to be Pareto optimal due to the augmentation term. A proof
of the optimality claim of (4.1) is provided in [17] on page 915. For further detail of (4.2)-(4.4)
see [8].
In addition to the solution generated by the diﬀerent subproblems, the DM may also ask for a
desired number of intermediate solutions generated between two Pareto optimal solutions. If so,
steps of equal length are taken in the decision space, and the corresponding objective vectors
are used as reference points in (4.3) [17].
A brief description of the basic steps in the synchronous NIMBUS algorithm is given below. The
set of saved solutions is denoted A and is initially set to A = ∅[17].
1. Generate a Pareto optimal starting solution.
2. Get the DM to classify the objective functions at the current solution according to the ﬁve
classes, along with possible aspiration levels and upper bounds.
3. Ask the DM to select the maximum number of new solutions to be generated (from one
to four) and choose subproblems to solve among (4.1)-(4.4).
4. Present the new solutions to the DM and ask if the person wants to save one or more
solutions to A. If so, include in A.
5. If the DM does not want to see intermediate solutions, go to step 7. Otherwise, ask the
DM to select two solutions in A and the number of intermediate solutions.
6. Generate the intermediate solutions and project them onto the Pareto frontier. Go to step
4.
7. Ask the DM to choose the most preferred solution from the set A and denote it as the
current solution. If the DM wants to continue, go to step 2; otherwise, stop.
The algorithm terminates when the DM is unwilling to let any objective functions increase or
does not need any of them to decrease. Otherwise, the search continues by iteratively moving
around the Pareto front.
4.2
ELECTRE
ELECTRE is a collection of outranking methodologies employed within the domain of MCDM.
The purpose of ELECTRE methods is to aid the DMs in identifying the smallest subset of
preferred alternatives from a set of options, considering multiple conﬂicting criteria. Let gj,
for j = 1, . . . , n denote a coherent family of criteria and let A denote the set of potential
alternatives; gj(a) reﬂect the evaluation of alternative a on criterion gj[4]. When modelling
real-world problems, one must be aware of the choice problem, which will inevitably shape the
outcome of the decision-making process.
B. Roy (1996) [43] distinguishes between four problematics (choice, sorting, ranking, and de-
scribing), of which ELECTRE methodologies address the ﬁrst three. However, all ELECTRE
methods are based on the same fundamental underlying concepts.
Modelling preferences in
ELECTRE is done by utilizing binary outranking relations, S, whose meaning is at least as good
as. Assessing two alternatives a and b, four situations may transpire[4].
• aSb and not bSa, i.e., a ≻b (a is preferred to b).
• bSa and not aSb, i.e., b ≻a (b is preferred to a).
==PAGE:32==
20
4.2. ELECTRE
• aSb and bSa, i.e, aIb (a is indiﬀerent to b).
• Not aSb and not bSa, i.e., aRb (a is incomparable to b).
The ELECTRE methods construct one or several outranking relations, which can be fuzzy,
crispy or embedded. The establishment of outranking relations is rooted in two fundamental
concepts:
• Concordance. A decisive majority of criteria should favour the assertion that an outranking
relation aSb should be validated.
• Non-discordance.
If the concordance criteria are satisﬁed, none of the criteria in the
minority should strongly contradict the assertion aSb.
For the outranking relation of aSb to be validated, both conditions must be fulﬁlled.
The
discordance of a criterion gj represents the veto eﬀect that the criterion exerts against the
assertion aiSjbh. ELECTRE methods follow the same structure, which is characterized by two
main procedures[4]:
• A multiple criteria aggregation procedure, enabling constructing one or several outranking
relations.
• An exploitation procedure generates results based on the choice problematic.
ELECTRE methods use discriminating thresholds to account for the imperfect nature of eval-
uating alternatives and the potential arbitrariness when constructing a family of criteria [4].
Consequently, this leads to a pseudo-criterion model for each criterion. Refer to section 4.2.2
for more details.
The discriminating thresholds are predominately used to model situations
when the relative performances aﬃliated with two distinct alternatives on a given criterion may
either[4]:
• Be compatible with indiﬀerence between the two alternatives(qj,indiﬀerence threshold).
• Warrant the preference of one of the two alternatives(pj, preference threshold).
• Be understood as wavering between choosing a preference for one alternative or indiﬀerence
between the two alternatives.
The thresholds (pj ≥qj ≥0) can be constant or vary along the scale of each criterion.
If
they are variable, one must distinguish between direct (performance of the worst alternative is
considered) and inverse (computed by utilizing the best performances)[4].
4.2.1
ELECTRE TRI
ELECTRE TRI is a sorting method designed to assign alternatives from a ﬁnite set A to
predeﬁned and ordered categories [44]. An upper and lower proﬁle characterizes each category.
The assignment of an alternative a to a suitable predeﬁned category should rely on intrinsic
values of a rather than comparing a with other alternatives from the A [45].
When assigning alternatives, the ELECTRE TRI method, like many other outranking meth-
ods, utilizes pseudo-criteria and various input parameter values provided by DMs through an
elicitation process [4]. The parameters can be segmented into preference parameters (weights,
thresholds, and proﬁles) and technical parameters(veto threshold and lambda-cutting levels)
[44]. The elicitation process is generally considered among the most challenging problems in
MCDA, as MCDA methods depend on mathematical models, which necessitates that prefer-
ences are expressed in mathematical terms [44].
==PAGE:33==
4.2. ELECTRE
21
Various elicitation techniques can be employed, but a holistic approach is favourable to reduce
the cognitive eﬀort for the DMs [45]. This is achieved by eliciting the parameters indirectly
through comprehensive information provided by DMs utilizing assignment examples a priori
[43]. An alternative approach developed in a study by Ramezanian (2019) [46] was initially
to elicit preference a posteriori. First, the expert assigns alternatives to predeﬁned categories,
where the proﬁles and thresholds are subsequently determined by a mixed-integer nonlinear
programming model[46].
ELECTRE TRI, as one of the later iterations of the ELECTRE methods, is recognized as the
most successful and frequently utilized method for MCDA sorting problems [44].
4.2.2
Presentation of the ELECTRE TRI method
In what follows, it is assumed that all criteria are to be maximized. Moreover, the notation as
described in [44] is used:
• F = {g1, · · · , gj, · · · , gn} Is the set of criteria. Let J denote the set of criterion indices.
• A = {a1, · · · , ai, · · · , am} is the set of alternatives. Let I denote the set of alternative
indices.
• C = {C1, · · · , Ch, · · · , Ck} is the set of categories.
It is listed in ascending preference
order. Therefore, C1 is considered the "worst" category. Let C denote the set of category
indices.
• B = {b1, · · · , bh, · · · , bk−1} is the set of proﬁles. The proﬁle bh is the upper limit of category
Ch and the lower limit of category Ch+1, for all h ∈B, where B is the set of proﬁle indices.
• w = {w1, · · · , wj, · · · , wn} is the weight vector modelling the preferences of DMs.
• gj(ai) is the performance of alternative ai on criterion gj for all i ∈I and j ∈J .
• M is the performance matrix composed of gj(ai) for all i ∈I and j ∈J .
The following exhaustive binary relations are used to facilitate the comparison of ai and bh:
• P is the strict preference relation, which is aiPbh denoting the relation "ai is strictly
preferred over bh".
• I is the indiﬀerence relation, which is aiIbh denoting the relation "ai is indiﬀerent to bh".
• Q is the weak preference relation, which is aiQbh denoting the relation "ai is weakly
preferred over bh".
• R is the incomparability relation, which is aiRbh denoting the relation that ai and bh are
incomparable.
• S is the outranking relation, which is aiSbh denoting the relation "ai is at least as good as
bh".
• ≻is the preference, both weak and strict relation.
The thresholds in ELECTRE are represented as follows:
• qj is the indiﬀerence threshold for the criterion gj.
q = (q1, · · · , qn) is the vector of
indiﬀerence thresholds.
• pj is the preference threshold for the criterion gj. p = (p1, · · · , pn) is the vector of prefer-
ence thresholds.
==PAGE:34==
22
4.2. ELECTRE
• vj is the veto threshold for the criterion gj. v = (v1, · · · , vn) is the vector of veto thresholds.
As stated earlier, ELECTRE TRI uses pseudo-criterion models for each criterion. A pseudo-
criterion is deﬁned as a function gj aﬃliated with two threshold functions q(gj(.)) and p(gj(.))
satisfying the following conditions, for all a, b ∈A [43]:
q(gj(b)) −q(gj(a)) ≥qj(a) −qj(b)
p(gj(b)) −p(gj(a)) ≥qj(a) −qj(b),
as well as ensuring that, for all a, b ∈A with gj(a) ≥gj(b):
aIjb ⇔gj(a) ≤gj(b) + q(gj(b))
aQjb ⇔gj(b) + q(gj(b)) < gj(a) ≤p(gj(b))
aPjb ⇔gj(b) + p(gj(b)) < gj(a)

1. The partial concordance indices are determined in the following manner, for all i ∈I and

4.3. RATIONALE FOR METHODOLOGICAL CHOICES
23
Transforming a fuzzy relation into a crisp one
When the credibility index is determined, the λ−cutting level has to be chosen, which is then
utilized to convert the fuzzy outranking relation into a crisp one[44]:
ρ(ai, bh) ≥λ ⇒aiSbh
(4.9)
ρ(ai, bh) < λ ⇒¬aiSbh
(4.10)
ρ(bh, ai) ≥λ ⇒bhSai
(4.11)
ρ(bh, ai) < λ ⇒¬bhSai
(4.12)
(4.13)
The λ represents the aggregation of weights of the group of criteria required to support the
assertion aiSbh and should fall in the range [0.5,1].
The exploitation procedure
The binary outranking relations are exploited in the exploitation procedure to allocate alterna-
tives to categories.
Comparing alternatives with proﬁles
The alternative ai and the proﬁle bh can be compared utilizing the acquired relationships. The
fuzzy relationships can be broken down into crisp relations as follows:
1. aiIbh ⇔aiSbh ∧bhSai
2. ai ≻bh ⇔aiSbh ∧¬bhSai
3. bh ≻ai ⇔¬aiSbh ∧bhSai
4. aiRbh ⇔¬aiSbh ∧¬bhSai
The sorting process of allocating alternatives to categories builds upon two well-established pro-
cedures: the conjunctive and disjunctive[4]. Based on these approaches, two possible exploita-
tion rules emerge: The pessimistic and the optimistic. Introducing two additional "proﬁles",
b0 (where every alternative is preferred) and bk(which is preferred over all alternatives), and
denoting the dominance relation as ∆, the dominance relation and the proﬁles can be connected
in the following manner[44]:
bk∆bk−1∆· · · ∆bh∆· · · ∆b1∆b0
Therefore, the two possible procedures for allocating alternatives to categories are:
The pessimistic procedure: An action ai is successively compared with bk, bk−1, · · · , until
aiSbk−1. Then ai is assigned to the best category Ch in a way that aiSbh−1.
The optimistic procedure:
An action ai is successively compared with b0, b1, · · · , until
bh ≻ai. Then ai is assigned to the worst category Ch in a way that bh ≻a
4.3
Rationale for Methodological Choices
The development of the decision-making approach is based on the following ideas, which aim to
address many of the identiﬁed areas of concern.
To help mitigate the ﬁrst area of concern, the interactive method, synchronous NIMBUS, is
chosen. The combinatorial complexity is reduced as only regions of interest are explored, thus
oﬀsetting the need to generate the entire Pareto front. Furthermore, it alleviates the require-
ment of the DM to have a complete understanding of the decision problem before the solution
process.
Moreover, it enables the DM(s) to learn about the decision problem and reiterate
==PAGE:36==
24
4.3. RATIONALE FOR METHODOLOGICAL CHOICES
their preferences accordingly. Furthermore, the NIMBUS method has been frequently applied
to real-life problems [16].
To address the second area of concern, the MCDM method ELECTRE TRI is chosen. The prefer-
ence elicitation process of ELECTRE TRI allows for the representation of multiple perspectives,
providing an overview of diﬀerent solution outcomes based on DM preferences. Articulating the
preference proﬁles may also reveal that DMs within the stakeholder group share common goals
and aspirations.
A combination of Synchronous NIMBUS and ELECTRE TRI is made to tackle the third area
of concern. This hybridization is thought to mitigate the potential numerous interactions be-
tween the DMs and the algorithm through an automation process. The automation process
will leverage a potential synergy between synchronous NIMBUS’s classiﬁcation of the respective
objectives and ELECTRI TRI’s categorization of solutions to guide the decision-making process,
potentially reducing the cognitive burden of involved stakeholders and the resources required to
achieve a desired outcome.
Lastly, the fourth area of concern will not be addressed in the master’s thesis. However, the
implications of this omission will be discussed in chapter 8.
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 5
Proposed Framework
The synchronous NIMBUS and the ELECTRE-TRI methods each have advantages and chal-
lenges, as detailed in chapter 4. One of the most prominent advantages of the synchronous NIM-
BUS method is its user-centric and ﬂexible nature. Focusing interactively on decision-makers
preferences can eﬀectively adjust the search for desired solutions based on new insights or chang-
ing priorities. However, this approach requires intensive engagement from decision-makers in
each iteration, which can be challenging in cases with limited resources. This limitation can
aﬀect stakeholders ability to articulate their preferences accurately and continuously. Regarding
the ELECTRE-TRI method, a central advantage is the establishment of stakeholder preference
proﬁles and evaluation possibilities. Considering both concordance and discordance indices, i.e.,
supportive and opposing evidence, when determining outranking relations ensures a detailed
analysis of the alternatives. However, this also poses a challenge due to the complexity of pa-
rameter setting. It can be diﬃcult to interpret how the parameters inﬂuence the outranking
relations and, consequently, the ﬁnal categorization.
The proposed framework integrates these two methods to exploit their advantages and address
their challenges. The complete framework will be presented in section 5.1. The main component
of the framework is the NIMBUS-ELECTRE Integrated Multi-Objective (NEIMO) algorithm,
which will be presented and explained in detail in section 5.2.
5.1
Integrating NIMBUS and ELECTRE-TRI in a Combined
Framework
The proposed combined framework is an interactive framework that allows for continuous re-
ﬁnement of the preference proﬁles and the search area outlined in the ﬁgure below.
==PAGE:38==
26 5.1. INTEGRATING NIMBUS AND ELECTRE-TRI IN A COMBINED FRAMEWORK
Figure 5.1: Outline of the proposed framework
The following subsections provide a general overview of the diﬀerent steps of the framework.
5.1.1
Step 1: Deﬁne problem and criteria
The ﬁrst and most crucial step is to deﬁne the problem and establish the corresponding criteria.
This involves specifying requirements and limitations and, in collaboration with the stakeholders,
assigning value functions to the criteria, which relate to measurable attributes reﬂecting the
criteria’s performance.
5.1.2
Step 2: Build mathematical optimization model
In this step, the problem is formulated as a mathematical model. This includes deﬁning decision
variables, expressing the criteria as objective functions, and constructing constraints based on
the problem limitations and requirements identiﬁed in the previous step. The relevant data is
collected and structured in this step for further analysis.
==PAGE:39==
5.1. INTEGRATING NIMBUS AND ELECTRE-TRI IN A COMBINED FRAMEWORK 27
5.1.3
Step 3: Approximate the Pareto front and estimate the nadir point
This step involves generating a set of well-diversiﬁed, non-dominated solutions that approximate
the Pareto front. The non-dominated solutions are used in the algorithm to explore new areas of
the solution space periodically and to estimate the nadir point, which is crucial in the NEIMO
algorithm. The methods for estimating the nadir point have been outlined in section 3.2.7.
Further, this step can help assess if non-conﬂicting objectives need to be addressed.
There exist diﬀerent methods for approximating the Pareto front, including MOEAs such as
NSGA-II, SPEA2 or other evolutionary algorithms1, as well as exact methods such as ε-constraint
methods, weighted-sum methods or the anytime algorithm, TPA. What is crucial is to obtain a
well-diversiﬁed set of Pareto optimal solutions within a reasonable time.
5.1.4
Step 4: Establish ELECTRE-TRI stakeholder preference proﬁles
The stakeholders are involved in developing preference proﬁles used in the ELECTRE-TRI
methodology, which includes criteria weights, proﬁle values, and thresholds. This step is es-
sential and will provide the basis for the search for solutions, therefore putting eﬀorts into it
is emphasized. As outlined in section 4.2.1, an analyst’s main challenge when engaging with
stakeholders is eﬀectively eliciting the various parameters that reﬂect the stakeholders subjective
preferences.
A possible approach is to elicit the parameters indirectly using holistic information gathered
from the stakeholders through assignment examples [47].
To support the collection of this
information:
• Stakeholders can be presented with essential information about candidate solutions from
the approximated Pareto front to help them understand the trade-oﬀs and possibilities.
• Structured surveys or questionnaires can be utilized.
• Interviews and focus group discussions can be conducted to gather in-depth insights.
5.1.5
Step 5: Run the NEIMO algorithm
In this step, the proposed NEIMO algorithm is executed. This algorithm integrates ELECTRE-
TRI methodologies into the synchronous NIMBUS reclassiﬁcation and update of reference point
procedures. The algorithm is detailed in section 5.2.
5.1.6
Step 6: Present the solutions to the stakeholders
When the algorithm terminates after ﬁnding a speciﬁed number of solutions, the results are pre-
sented to the stakeholders for evaluation. This step involves organizing meetings or workshops
to present the solutions and gather stakeholder feedback. This step helps the stakeholders to in-
terpret the trade-oﬀs of their preferences, including how the parameters inﬂuence the outranking
relations.
5.1.7
Step 7: Stakeholder satisfaction assessment
This step assesses whether the stakeholders are satisﬁed with the presented solutions. If not,
the preference proﬁles, model constraints and bounds are recalibrated based on their feedback
and the NEIMO algorithm is re-run.
5.1.8
Step 8: Conduct ﬁnal assessment and choose the best solution
A ﬁnal assessment is performed to select the best solution. This can be based on other MCDA
tools, Cost-Beneﬁt Analysis (CBA), risk assessments, sensitivity analysis, or a combination of
these methods.
1See literature review section 3.2.6 for examples of state-of-the-art methods.
==PAGE:40==
28 5.2. NIMBUS-ELECTRE INTEGRATED MULTI-OBJECTIVE (NEIMO) ALGORITHM
The proposed framework integrates the NIMBUS and ELECTRE-TRI methods for multi-objective
optimization. The framework ensures alignment with stakeholder values by involving stakehold-
ers in deﬁning the problem and criteria, establishing preference proﬁles, and reviewing candidate
solutions. The iterative process of the NEIMO algorithm dynamically updates classiﬁcations
and reference points. Final solutions are selected based on comprehensive evaluations, including
MCDA tools, CBA, and risk assessments, ensuring robust decision-making. This structured ap-
proach ensures the optimization process is adaptive and responsive to stakeholder preferences,
leading to more well-informed and balanced decisions.
5.2
NIMBUS-ELECTRE Integrated Multi-Objective (NEIMO)
algorithm
The main proposal is the NEIMO algorithm that searches for alternatives using the synchronous
NIMBUS method, wherein the reclassiﬁcation and update of aspiration levels in each iteration
are automated by utilizing the preference proﬁles and categorizations of the ELECTRE-TRI
methodology. The algorithm is presented in Algorithm 1, and all steps are illustrated in ﬁgure
5.2.
==PAGE:41==
5.2. NIMBUS-ELECTRE INTEGRATED MULTI-OBJECTIVE (NEIMO) ALGORITHM 29

Figure 5.2: An overview of the diﬀerent processes in the NEIMO algorithm.
5.2.1
Input and output
The algorithm inputs consist of the following elements: The parameters, pm, and sets, st,
correspond to the parameters of the Mixed-Integer Programming model. The upper bound,
==PAGE:43==
5.2. NIMBUS-ELECTRE INTEGRATED MULTI-OBJECTIVE (NEIMO) ALGORITHM 31
zupper, is the upper limit of the objectives classiﬁed to be in I⩾. The augmentation coeﬃcient,
ρ, for the scalarizing functions of the synchronous NIMBUS, explained in section 4.1, ensures
Pareto optimality [17]. The parameters in the container, NIMBUSparams, correspond to the
parameters for the ELECTRE-TRI method, which include stakeholder criteria weights, proﬁles,
and thresholds.
PF contains a set of non-dominated solutions that approximate the entire
Pareto front. The parameters TI, TM, FD, and FI refer to the thresholds and increase/decrease
factors used for reclassifying objectives and updating reference points. N is the target number
of solutions to be found.
The algorithm’s output is the set of solutions (A). All solutions are in the ELECTRE-TRI
category c3.
5.2.2
Step 1: Initialization
The algorithm initializes the parameters for the NIMBUS method and sets up initial probability
weights and other required variables. This is done through a separate initialization procedure,
as detailed in Algorithm 2.
Algorithm 2 Initialization function
1: function initialize(pm, st, zupper, ρ, ELECTREparams, PF, TI, TM, FD, FI)
2:
Input: MIP-model parameters and sets (pm, st), upper bounds (zupper), augmentation

4:

◃Initialize empty solution structure
7:

◃Assign equal weights for thresholds and factors
8:
class ←[ ]
◃Initialize classiﬁcations
9:
(zideal, znadir) ←Extract the ideal and nadir points from PF
10:
zutopian ←zideal −0.01 · zideal
11:
zaspire ←randomParetoFrontPoint(PF)
12:
zcurrent ←zaspire
◃Set the current solution to the initial reference point
13:
Z ←[zideal, znadir, zutopian, zcurrent, zaspire, zupper]
14:
NIMBUSparams ←[ρ, Z, class]
◃Initialize the NIMBUS parameters
15:
sol ←solveAchievementProblem(st, pm,NIMBUSparams)
16:
if pessimistic then
◃Choose ELECTRE-TRI method for categorization
17:

18:
else
19:

20:
end if
21:
if sol ∈c[3] then
22:

23:
end if

25:
l ←{l ∈{1, 2, 3}| sol ∈c[l]}
◃The category l of the solution sol
26:
profile ←The proﬁle dividing the category l and l + 1
