As explained in the section 4.1, the NIMBUS procedure needs an initial classiﬁcation and initial
==PAGE:44==
32 5.2. NIMBUS-ELECTRE INTEGRATED MULTI-OBJECTIVE (NEIMO) ALGORITHM
reference points. The initialization procedure consists of the following steps:
1. Initialize: Initialize solution structure (A), probability weights (wTI, wTM , wFD, wFI),
empty classiﬁcation set (class), and the diﬀerent NIMBUS objective parameters (Z). No-
tice that a random point from the Pareto front is used as the initial aspiration level
(zaspire).
2. Solve: Solve one of the scalarizing subproblems to get the initial solution, e.g., the achieve-
ment subproblem.
3. Categorize:
Categorize the initial solution (sol) either by the pessimistic or optimistic
procedure. If the solution is in category c3, save it to A.
4. Update NIMBUS parameters: Classify the objectives utilizing the Algorithm 3 and
update the reference points by Algorithm 5. Update the NIMBUS parameters accordingly.
5.2.3
Step 2: Choose exploitation or exploration
The algorithm decides with a speciﬁc probability whether to explore new solutions or exploit the
current solution. If the mode is set to exploit, the algorithm solves all scalarizing problems (4.1)-
(4.4) using the current parameters to obtain new solutions (sol). Suppose the mode, however, is
set to explore. In that case, the algorithm selects a random point from the Pareto front as the
aspiration level (zaspire) and solves the achievement problem (4.3) to obtain new solutions (sol).
5.2.4
Step 3: Categorize the solutions
The solutions are categorized using the ELECTRE-TRI procedure. Depending on the chosen
procedure (pessimistic or optimistic), the solutions are categorized in c. Solutions classiﬁed in
category c3 are stored in the set of solutions (A).
5.2.5
Step 4: Selection for next iteration
If solutions are in category c2, one is randomly selected for the next iteration’s current solution.
Otherwise, a solution from category c1 is chosen. The current objective values (zcurrent) are
updated with the selected solution’s values.
5.2.6
Step 5: Update probability weights and indices
The probability weights for thresholds and factors are updated based on the current ELECTRE
categorization, and the index for thresholds and factors is selected using the updated probability
weights.
5.2.7
Step 6: Reclassify objectives
Objectives are reclassiﬁed using the procedure of Algorithm 3 based on the selected ELECTRE-
TRI proﬁle and thresholds.
5.2.8
Step 7: Update of reference points
The aspiration levels (zaspire) are updated using the procedure of Algorithm 5 based on the new
classiﬁcation and selected factors.
5.2.9
Step 8: Termination criterion
The algorithm terminates when ﬁnding the desired number of solutions (N).
The NEIMO algorithm integrates the NIMBUS and ELECTRE-TRI methods to search multi-
objective solutions iteratively. Dynamically updating classiﬁcations and reference points ade-
quately identiﬁes optimal solutions within the DM’s predeﬁned preferences. In the exploitation
phase, it solves scalarizing problems to reﬁne the current solution. In contrast, the exploration
phase introduces variety by solving an achievement problem with randomly selected Pareto
==PAGE:45==
5.2. NIMBUS-ELECTRE INTEGRATED MULTI-OBJECTIVE (NEIMO) ALGORITHM 33
front points. This approach enhances multi-objective optimization by providing an algorithm
for balancing solution reﬁnement and discovery.
The following sections will introduce how the ELECTRE-TRI measures can be utilized to re-
classify objectives and update the NEIMO method’s reference points. Finally, a procedure is
proposed to select appropriate values for the thresholds and factors.
5.2.10
Reclassiﬁcation of objectives using ELECTRE-TRI
In the synchronous NIMBUS method, the reclassiﬁcation of objectives is done iteratively in
close collaboration with the stakeholders until they are satisﬁed with the set of alternatives.
This process can be resource-intensive and is not always desirable. However, instead of asking
the stakeholders to reclassify the objectives, the ELECTRE-TRI preference proﬁles, deﬁned a
priori, is used as an alternative. The complete reclassiﬁcation procedure is outlined in Algorithm
3 and is implemented in B.5.2.
Algorithm 3 Reclassify function
1: function reclassify(ELECTREparams, profile, v, tI, tM)
2:
Input: ELECTRE-TRI parameters (ELECTREparams), threshold proﬁle (profile),
3:
value scores of the solution (v), threshold values (tI, tM)
4:
Output: NIMBUS classiﬁcations class
5:
Initialize:
6:
improve, maintain, degrade ←[ ]
◃Placeholder for initial classiﬁcations
7:
I<, I⩽, I=, I⩾, I⋄←[ ]
◃Classes for ﬁnal classiﬁcations
8:
e ←0
◃Initialize discordance/concordance eﬀect measure
9:
for each objective i in v do
10:
con ←concordance(v[i], profile, ELECTREparams)
11:
dis ←discordance(v[i], profile, ELECTREparams)
12:
e[i] ←ELECTREparams.w[i] · (dis −con)
13:
if e[i] ≥tI then
◃tI is the lower bound for the improve class
14:
Push objective i to the improve class
15:
else if e[i] ≤tM then
◃tM is the upper bound for the maintain class
16:
Add objective i to the maintain class
17:
else
18:
Add objective i to the degrade class
19:
end if
20:
end for
21:
improve, maintain, degrade ←checkFeasibility(e, improve, maintain, degrade)
22:
for each objective j in improve do
23:
Randomly add objective j to either I< or I⩽class
24:
end for
25:
for each objective j in degrade do
26:
Randomly add objective j to either I⩾or I⋄class
27:
end for
28:
I= ←maintain
29:
return class = [I<, I⩽, I=, I⩾, I⋄]
30: end function
The function in Algorithm 3 inputs the value scores of the solution, the preference proﬁle of the
stakeholder, the threshold proﬁle, and tI and tM values used in equation (5.2). The output is a
feasible NIMBUS classiﬁcation of the objectives that can be used in the synchronous NIMBUS
==PAGE:46==
34 5.2. NIMBUS-ELECTRE INTEGRATED MULTI-OBJECTIVE (NEIMO) ALGORITHM
procedure. Notice, in lines 22-28, the objectives are placed into the ﬁve original NIMBUS classes,
I<, I⩽, I=, I⩾, and I⋄, at random to broaden the search for new solutions.
The main idea of Algorithm 3 is to utilize a weighted diﬀerence in discordance and concordance
indices as a measure of the performance of each objective. As explained in section 4.2.2, the
concordance index coni(a, b) indicates the degree to which criterion i supports the preference
of alternative a over threshold proﬁle b. Conversely, the discordance index disi(a, b) indicates
the degree to which criterion i opposes the preference of alternative a over threshold proﬁle
b.
The diﬀerence disi(a, b) −coni(a, b) for each criterion i highlights the net eﬀect of that
criterion in supporting or opposing alternative a in the outranking of a proﬁle b. Further, as
part of eliciting preference proﬁles, stakeholders are asked to assign a weight wi to each criterion
i. Combining these measures leads to a weighted measure of the eﬀect ei(a, b) of criterion i
comparing alternative a and the threshold proﬁle b while accounting for the criterion weight wi.
That is,
ei(a, b) = wi (disi(a, b) −coni(a, b))
(5.1)
where ei(a, b) > 0 indicates that the criterion i more strongly opposes the preference of alter-
native a, and ei(a, b) < 0 indicates that the criterion i more strongly supports the preference of
the alternative a.
The NIMBUS classes, detailed in section 4.1, include categories where objectives are desired to
be improved, maintained at the current level, or degraded. The aim is to determine which class
each objective i should belong to. By deﬁning some threshold values tI and tM, the eﬀect ei(a, b)
can be utilized in this classiﬁcation process. The reclassiﬁcation can then be expressed as,
classi =
⎧
⎪
⎨
⎪
⎩
improve
if ei(a, b) ≥tI,
maintain
if ei(a, b) ≤tM,
degrade
otherwise
(5.2)
where tI > 0 is the lower bound of falling into improve class, and tM < 0 is the upper bound of
falling into maintain class.
• ei(a, b) ≥tI suggests objective i has a negative impact on the assessment of a and therefore
needs improvement.
• ei(a, b) ≤tM indicates objective i performs well and does not require changes
• tM < ei(a, b) < tI implies the objective i performs neither particularly well nor poorly and
could be allowed to degrade to focus on more critical objectives.
==PAGE:47==
5.2. NIMBUS-ELECTRE INTEGRATED MULTI-OBJECTIVE (NEIMO) ALGORITHM 35
Algorithm 4 Check feasibility of classiﬁcation
1: function checkFeasibility(e, improve, maintain, degrade)
2:
Input: Eﬀect measure (e), initial classes (improve, maintain, degrade)
3:
Output:
Feasible classiﬁcation classes improve, maintain, degrade
4:
if improve = ∅and ||degrade|| > 1 then
5:
Add the objective i where ei(a, b) = maxj∈degrade(ej(a, b)) to improve
6:
Remove i from degrade
7:
else if improve = ∅and ||degrade|| = 1 then
8:
Add the objective i where ei(a, b) = maxj∈maintain(ej(a, b)) to improve
9:
Remove i from maintain
10:
else if degrade = ∅and maintain ̸= ∅then
11:
Add the objective i where ei(a, b) = maxj∈maintain(ej(a, b)) to degrade
12:
Remove i from maintain
13:
else if degrade = ∅and ||improve|| > 1 then
14:
Add the objective i where ei(a, b) = minj∈improve(ej(a, b)) to degrade
15:
Remove i from improve
16:
end if
17: end function
Additionally, the reclassiﬁcation of objectives has to be feasible by the deﬁnition of a feasible
classiﬁcation: I< ∪I⩽̸= ∅and I⩾∪I⋄̸= ∅presented in section 4.1. The improve and degrade
classes must not be empty.
A procedure to make sure that the classiﬁcation is feasible is
presented in Algorithm 4 and is implemented in Julia (see Appendix B.5.2).
5.2.11
Updating aspiration levels using ELECTRE-TRI categorizations
Apart from reclassifying the objectives, stakeholders are also asked to iteratively adjust the
reference points based on learnings from previous iterations. This task can be automated by
considering the reclassiﬁed objectives in class and applying decrease factor fD and increase
factor fI. Algorithm 5 outlines the procedure for updating the reference points.
Algorithm 5 Function for Updating the Reference Points
1: function UpdateReferencePoints(rcurr, class, fD, fI)
2:
Input: Current reference points (rcurr), NIMBUS classiﬁcation (class), increase/de-
crease factors (fD, fI)
3:
Output: Updated reference points rnew
4:
Initialize rnew ←0
5:
for each objective i in rcurr do
6:
if i ∈class[1] or i ∈class[2] then
◃Objective is in I< or I⩽
7:
rnew[i] ←(1 −fD) · rcurr[i]
◃Decrease reference value
8:
else if i ∈class[4] or i ∈class[5] then
◃Objective is in I⩾or I⋄
9:
rnew[i] ←(1 + fI) · rcurr[i]
◃Increase reference value
10:
else
11:
rnew[i] ←rcurr[i]
◃Maintain current reference
12:
end if
13:
end for
14:
return rnew
15: end function
The procedure of Algorithm 5 takes the current reference points, the classiﬁcation of objectives,

can be used in the synchronous NIMBUS approach.

reference point rcurr

5.2.12
Selecting appropriate values for thresholds and factors
It is essential to consider how to determine the actual values of the thresholds tI, tM and the
decrease and increase factors fD, fI as they can signiﬁcantly impact the search for diverse and
reasonable solutions. Each problem instance is diﬀerent, and choosing a static set of parameters
might not ﬁt all cases. An approach to tackle this problem, greatly inspired by the Adaptive
Large Neighborhood Search (ALNS) algorithm, presented in [48], is to consider several values
for each parameter and choose the values based on dynamically updated probabilities.
In ALNS, this approach is used to choose between several repair and destroy methods for the
heuristic algorithm by assigning a weight to each method. The weights are adjusted dynamically
based on the performance of the methods, and the selection of subsequent destroy and repair
methods is done using a roulette wheel principle. The probability of choosing each method is
calculated based on their weights. This principle allows the heuristic to adapt to the instance
and the search state [48].
This principle is adopted in the NEIMO algorithm by deﬁning a set of threshold values TI, and
TM, where TI is a vector containing diﬀerent lower bounds of falling into improve class, and TM
is a vector containing diﬀerent bounds of falling into the maintain class. Similarly, for the set of
factors FD and FI containing diﬀerent decrease and increase factors, respectively. Each element
in the vectors is assigned a weight and a probability, e.g. the element fj
D ∈FD is assigned a
weight wj
FD, and the probability φj
FD. The probability is calculated as follows,
φj
FD =
wj
FD
"|FD|
k=1 wk
FD
(5.4)
The weights are updated dynamically according to the recorded performance of the threshold
and factor values. A performance score ψ is introduced that considers the ELECTRE category
of a given solution and a speciﬁc combination of parameters from TI, TM, FD and FI that has
been used to generate the solution. The score is determined by,
ψ =
⎧
⎪
⎨
⎪
⎩
10
if the solution is in category c3,
5
if the solution is in category c2,
0
if the solution is in category c1.
(5.5)
A high value of ψ corresponds to a good solution, and vice versa. This measure is used to
update the weights. Considering a case where fj
D has been used in the previous iteration, the
corresponding weight wj
FD is updated as follows,
wj
FD = λwj
FD + (1 −λ)ψ
(5.6)
==PAGE:49==
5.2. NIMBUS-ELECTRE INTEGRATED MULTI-OBJECTIVE (NEIMO) ALGORITHM 37
where λ ∈[0, 1] is the decay parameter that controls how sensitive the weight is to changes in
the performance of the chosen value [48]. Updating all weights used in the previous iteration
follows the same calculations as in equations (5.4)−(5.6).
The explained approach of dynamically choosing the threshold values and factor values is im-
plemented in Julia and used in the NEIMO algorithm.
==PAGE:50==
Chapter 6
Distribution of High School Students
Case Study
In the following chapter, the proposed framework will be applied to a large combinatorial multi-
objective problem: the distribution of high school students in the capital region of Denmark.
The chapter follows the structure of the framework, with each step explained in detail.
6.1
Case Description
Each year in Denmark, thousands of young people apply for admission to Upper Secondary
Education (USE) program at their preferred institutions, with the Higher General Examination
Program (stx) being the largest among them [49]. This entails a huge combinatorial problem
for the Danish ministry of Children and Education, which administrates the distribution and
admission of coming pupils to the respective institutions. Since 2023, a central distribution sys-
tem has been utilized to carry out this resource-intensive task. Previously, the tasks was carried
out in a decentralized matter by the regions. In recent times, there has been an increasing inter-
est among the public and politicians in Denmark regarding the establishment, facilitation, and
maintenance of optimal upper secondary education for the youth. In this context, it is notewor-
thy to shed light on the following high school reforms and adjustments and their most impactful
changes. The 2016 reform [50] aimed to revamp the introductory phase and to provide clearer
academic guidance and a better start for pupils in high school. This would be encompassed by
the following initiatives:
• The foundation course period was reduced to three months.
• The number of specialized study programs was drastically reduced
• Pupils should not express preference in terms of specialized study programs prior to ad-
mission.
The main purpose of the 2021 [51] reform was to mitigate increasing tendency of pupils seg-
regating into clusters based on their social, cultural, and ethnic background. In practice, the
country would be segmented into two distinct zones, either a distance zone or a distribution
zone, with the latter zone having the most profound impact on the distribution. In the distance
zone, pupils are admitted based on priority and distance to said institutions, whereas in the dis-
tribution zone, priority, distance and parents’ income level are the determinant factors. Larger
cities and municipalities, such as the Capital Region of Denmark, would be distribution zones.
Parental average income would be divided into three levels across the country.
==PAGE:51==
6.1. CASE DESCRIPTION
39
• Low-income group: Parental income lower than the 1st income quartile.
• Middle-income group: Parental income between the 1st and 3rd income quartiles.
• High-income group: Parental income higher than the 3rd income quartile.
The calculations based on 2020 applicants would yield the following parental income boundaries,
rounded to the nearest 25.000 DKK [51]:
• Boundary between low and middle income group: Approximately 300.000 DKK
• Boundary between middle and high income group: Approximately 500.000 DKK
In late 2022, the government rolled back the agreement on pupil distribution from 2021, removing
the criterion of parental income in pupil distribution to high schools [52]. Instead, priority and
transportation time are now the determining factors in the distribution. However, the current
government are working on initiatives mitigating a uneven pupil composition. As of now, the
governance and procedure concerning high school pupil distribution operates as follows.
Applications for USE programs are registered on digital platform from January to March. Pupils
are able to prioritize any institution in Denmark. The pupil creates a lexicographic order of
priorities, priority 1 being their most wanted institution and USE program, with no maximum
nor minimum number of priorities. The governing rules are applicable the four national USE
programs (stx, hhx, htx and hf) [53]. Applicants submitting within the time frame, will get
reserved a provisional spot at an institution. However, it is not guaranteed at a prioritized
institution. The distribution of spots are based on the applications preferences, and sought to
be fulﬁlled in the order speciﬁed by the applicant. The applicant will get reserved a provisional
spot at their ﬁrst priority if:
• The institutions have more spots than applicants.
• The applicant are among the applicants with the lowest transportation time, if the insti-
tution has more applicants than spots.
If the applicant fails to get a provisional spot at their ﬁrst priority, it is assessed in a similar
matter, if the applicant can secure a spot at their second priority and so on. Applicants with
special requirements or interests, can get precedence at select institutions.
If precedence is
given, the applicant will reserve a provisional spot at their preferred institution in advance of
the rest. In the case that the applicant does not get a provisional spot at any of their prioritized
institutions, the central distribution system will attempt to allocate a spot at a institution with
capacity within 45 minutes of travel time from the applicant’s address, which has the same
USE program as the applicants ﬁrst priority. If this renders infeasible, the responsible region
will manually allocate a provisional spot at a institution to the applicant. To see the central
distribution allocation process depicted in a ﬂowchart, refer to ﬁgure 6.1.
==PAGE:52==
40
6.2. PREREQUISITES AND SIMPLIFICATIONS
Figure 6.1: AS-IS procedure of allocating pupils to high schools for USE programs.
6.2
Prerequisites and Simpliﬁcations
As it stands, there is an inherent lexicographic priority among objectives within the government’s
central distribution system. The criteria used in the central distribution system such as priority
and transportation time will be reﬂected in the mathematical model. Additionally, the diversity
criteria proposed by the government in 2021, will also be considered.
Until now, the criteria have been discussed on a cursory level. Therefore, the following three
sub-sections oﬀer context to their relevance and their integration into the mathematical model.
==PAGE:53==
6.2. PREREQUISITES AND SIMPLIFICATIONS
41
6.2.1
Priority
The priority criterion is implemented to provide pupils with the freedom to choose their preferred
USE program and institution, something paramount to the Danish USE culture. Through a
lexicographic ordering of priorities, the pupils can express their preferences in terms of the
institutions and USE programs. Thus, allowing the central distribution system to accommodate
the individual pupil’s preferences to the highest degree possible. The priority criteria will be
processed similarly to the central distribution system, with the exception that a maximum of
ﬁve priorities can be chosen. As the priority number increases from 1, the relative importance
of the institution and it’s department (institutions can have multiple departments oﬀering USE
programs) decreases. If an institution is assigned the priority number 6, that means that the
particular institution and it’s department is of no priority to the pupil.
6.2.2
Transportation time
The inclusion of transportation time as a criterion was a political decision based on a close
proximity principle. The principle advocates for pupils to be able to access local USE institu-
tions, as well as providing a more transparent decision-making process for selecting the most
appropriate institutions for the individual pupil. Moreover, using this principle as leverage to
sustain institutions in less populated areas of Denmark [54]. The estimated transportation time
for each pupil to a given institution is derived by utilizing a combination of the two following
open-source software[55].
For public transport, OpenTripPlanner (OTP) is used, and Open
Source Routing Machine (OSRM) is used for bicycle rides. Transportation can either by public
transport and walking, or by bicycle and walking. For a route to be eligible, the pupil must at
most arrive 30 minutes prior to opening hours, and no later than 5 minutes before opening hours
from their registered address in the Central Person Register. In the case, that no valid route can
be determined, the applicants receive an administrative travel time to a given institution of 23
minutes[55]. The applicant will be presented with the estimated travel time in minutes to each
of the prioritized institutions in the application process. Naturally, transportation time as an
objective will be included in the mathematical model. Within the model, transportation time
is interpreted similarly to how it is in the central distribution system, representing the shortest
travel time from a pupil’s address to a given institution in minutes.
6.2.3
Diversity
Diversity in this context, is deﬁned as the appropriate composition of pupils at an institution
based on the average parental income. The appropriate composition is set by the respective
distribution zone and should reﬂect the demographics of ﬁrst priority residents within the zone.
Distribution zones are clusters of institutions exhibiting a high deviation from the national
median parental income [56].
In 2021, the Ministry of Children and Education deﬁned ﬁve
distribution zones, three of them being within the Capital Region of Denmark. In practical
terms, the institutions within the distribution zones split their pupil capacity into three groups
reﬂecting the pupils’ average parental income level. The size of each group, measured by the
number of available spots, is dependent on the demographic composition of the respective dis-
tribution zone. Instead of allocating a speciﬁc capacity per income group, the mathematical
model seeks to minimize the deviational variables associated with the chosen aspiration levels.
The aspiration levels are derived by the demographic composition of the respective distribution
zone. Nevertheless, this approach will allow for a certain degree of ﬂexibility in terms of capacity
for each income level.
For the mathematical model to yield feasible solutions, the following prerequisites are accounted
for:
• All applicants must receive a provisional spot.
==PAGE:54==
42
6.3. CONSTRUCTION OF VALUE FUNCTIONS
• Applicant’s priorities and USE program are accounted for, ﬁrst priority being the pupil’s
most preferred.
• Applicant’s transportation time to a given institution is accounted for.
• Applicant’s parental income is accounted for as a diversity measure.
• The maximum capacity of provisional spots at each institution across USE programs is
accounted for.
• The pupil is allowed to choose multiple USE programs in the prioritization of institutions.
• If priority is not fulﬁlled, applicants can only be allocated to an institution with their
preferred USE-program.
The following assumptions are accounted for in the model:
1. All pupils will be allocated.
2. Transportation time is the minimum transportation time a pupil has to a given institution.
3. A pupil has at most ﬁve prioritized institutions.
4. It is assumed that the capacity of the institution and respective USE program can not be
exceeded at any point.
5. If a pupil is not allocated to any of their priorities, the pupil will be allocated to an
institution within 45 minutes of transportation time. Further, the USE program of this
institution will be of the same type as the ﬁrst priority of the pupil.
6. The diversity criterion is assumed to be assessed by the deviation from the targeted
parental income level distributions.
7. The model accounts for precedence in terms of proﬁle study lines, but omits handicap,
diagnosis, etc.
6.3
Construction of Value Functions
Based on the case description, model prerequisites, and simpliﬁcations, three criteria have been
identiﬁed for assessing solutions to the pupil distribution problem.
Due to data constraints
and limited access to stakeholders and decision-makers, constructing value functions for each
criterion necessitates simpliﬁcations and assumptions. However, an attempt has been made to
create value functions that approximate the potential preference structure of the DMs. The
construction process involves two steps: The ﬁrst entails estimating parameter input values,
followed by step 2, calculating an appropriate function. All value functions are construed on
a 0-100 scale to ensure comparability.
For each criterion, the value functions are evaluated
on diﬀerent levels; the priority and transport time value scores are calculated for each pupil,
whereas the diversity value score is calculated for each department. Each score is aggregated
into a total score to evaluate the alternatives.
Two mathematical concepts are introduced that are believed to capture the preference structure
of the DMs; a piecewise function for the priority criterion and sigmoid functions for transporta-
tion time and diversity criteria.
A piecewise function is a function in which the domain is
divided into subdomains, and given the discrete nature of the prioritization of institutions and
==PAGE:55==

Logistic functions are characterized by their S-shaped curve, which can assume diﬀerent growth
rates depending on certain thresholds. This characteristic is beneﬁcial to modelling the value

6.4
Mixed-integer programming model
In this section, a mixed-integer programming model is formulated that distributes applicants
from Denmark’s capital region among the various high schools in the area. Firstly, the model’s
sets, parameters, and constraints will be presented and explained. Hereafter, the diﬀerent ob-
jective functions are introduced and lastly, the complete model is presented.

6.4.5
Constraints
In mathematical optimization, constraints are conditions or restrictions speciﬁed on the variables
of an optimization problem. These constraints must be satisﬁed for a solution to be considered
feasible. The purpose of constraints is to deﬁne the feasible region, which is the set of all points
that satisfy the problem’s constraints.
Firstly, to guarantee that each pupil is allocated to a USE program, the subsequent constraint
mandates that every pupil must be assigned to precisely one program of the same type as their
preferred USE programs.
!
j∈J |uj∈Θi
xij = 1,
∀i ∈I
(6.8)
This constraint ensures that each student is matched to a USE program that is among their
preferences, where Θi represents the prioritized USE programs for student i.
==PAGE:59==
6.4. MIXED-INTEGER PROGRAMMING MODEL
47
Conversely, pupils cannot be assigned to USE programs for which they have not prioritized.
This requirement is encapsulated in the constraint below,
!

xij = 0,
∀i ∈I
(6.9)
This constraint ensures that for each pupil i, the sum of assignments to non-preferred programs
is zero.
It is critical to adhere to the capacity limits of each high school and its various USE programs.
The next constraint ensures that the number of students assigned to a USE program does not
exceed its maximum capacity.
!

xij ≤Qj,

(6.10)

number of students assigned is within the allowable capacity Qj.
To ensure the prosperity of the high schools, a minimum number of pupils in each high school
can be enforced. In fact, according to the Danish Ministry of Children and Education, there
must be at least 84 pupils in total at each high school [57]. Therefore the following constraint
is added to the model,

6.4.6
Objective functions
As outlined in the previous section, the multi-objective mathematical model is designed to man-
age multiple criteria and their associated trade-oﬀs. This section will formulate and describe an
objective function for each criterion. These separate functions will address applicants’ prefer-
ences individually, minimize travel time, and enhance income diversity.
The ﬁrst criterion to address is the alignment with the applicants’ preferences. If pijxij = 1, it
indicates that pupil i is placed in their most preferred USE program department j. On the other
hand, pijxij = 6 indicates that pupil i is assigned to a department that did not meet any of their
preference levels. Thus, to optimize the distribution of applicants according to their priorities,
the model seeks to minimize the sum of the products of priority levels and assignment decisions
across all pupils and departments, given by "
i∈I,j∈J pijxij.

(6.20)
This objective ensures that applicant distribution is proportionate to income diversity targets.
To ensure that the proﬁle applicants have priority on the respective schools, a reward term is
added to each objective function that provides a reward if a pupil is assigned a proﬁle study
line. The reward term will be on the form,
. . . + R
!
h∈H
!
i∈Ωh
yi(jh)
(6.21)
where R ∈R is a reward scalar scaled according to the diﬀerent objective functions of the model.
Notice that this term must be subtracted when evaluating the objective values to interpret the
objective function value realistically.
The following section will dive into the data gathering that is used in the MIP model presented
in section 6.4.
6.5
Data Gathering
Collecting the necessary personal information on the applicants, such as addresses, average
parental income, and their preferences, is inherently challenging.
This sensitive data might
conﬂict with the EU General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR). However, the master’s thesis
aims to develop a general framework that combines MOP and MCDA methods to assist decision-
makers in solving complex multi-criteria problems involving multiple stakeholders. This thesis
focuses on integrating these methods and the potential applications of the framework rather
than the actuality of the results. Subsequently, the case study serves as a proof-of-concept for
the framework. Given these limitations, generating additional applicant-speciﬁc data becomes
necessary to support the development.
Despite these challenges, general information about the distribution of students from previous
years has been available, including data on the various high schools, their capacities, the number
==PAGE:62==
50
6.5. DATA GATHERING
of applicants, the number of accepted students, the parental income aspiration levels of each
high school, and other related metrics.
This section will introduce the collected data, both the data used directly as parameters of
the model and the data used to generate a realistic representation of the sensitive applicant
information. Further, the shortcomings of the generated data will be discussed.
6.5.1
Collected data
The metrics have been gathered from [49], which lists the results from the 2023 pupil distribution,
and [58], which lists the parental income level distribution from the 2021 pupil distribution. Both
reports provide department-speciﬁc data.
The ﬁrst report contains the following information about each high school from the distribution
in 2023:
1) Department reference name
2) USE program
3) Student capacity
4) Number of ﬁrst-priority applicants
5) Number of distributed students
The second report details the parental income levels of the 2021 distribution:
6) Department reference name
7) The department’s parental income level percentages for 2020, divided into low, middle,
and high-income groups.3
8) The expected parental income level percentages of the 2021 distribution model, divided
into low, middle, and high-income groups.
Directly from these metrics, the sets I, J , S and K, the subsets Φs and Γ, and the parameters
N, M+, M, uj, Qj, Qmin and ajk can be extracted. The set I = {1, 2, . . . , N}, where N is
the total number of applicants determined by the sum of the number of distributed students in
2023, listed in item 5). The sets J and S are extracted from item 1), counting the total number
of department reference names, M+, and the distinct reference names, M, respectively. The
set K = {low, middle, high} reﬂects the income level groups. The subset of USE programs of
each distinct high school, Φs, and the USE program of each USE program department in J , uj,
are extracted from item 2). The capacities of each department, Qj, are given by item 3). The
subset of departments with income levels, Γ, is derived from item 6) and the target percentage of
pupils in the diﬀerent income groups, ajk, are interpreted as the expected income distributions
from item 8).
6.5.2
Generation of realistic data
The remaining parameters required in the model are speciﬁc to each applicant and must be
generated. These include the priorities of the applicants, pij, the income group to which the
applicants belong, dki, the travel times for each applicant to each high school, tij. Additionally,
the prioritized USE programs of each applicant Θi and the ﬁrst priority of each applicant, τi,
are derived from these metrics.
3See chapter 6.1 for description of the income groups.
==PAGE:63==
6.5. DATA GATHERING
51
Travel times, tij
As described in section 6.2.2, transportation times of the applicants are deﬁned as the shortest
travel time to each institution either from travelling on a bicycle or by a combination of public
transport and walking.
This model employs a similar approach and requires the addresses of the institutions and all
applicants. The institution addresses in terms of coordinates (latitude and longitude) is easily
obtained by Google Maps. However, the student coordinates have to be generated. To accom-
plish this, a Python script utilizing OpenStreetMap API is created to randomly assign addresses
to the applicants while complying with a proximity logic detailed below:
Each institution must have the number of pupils in close proximity (within 100 metres and 10
kilometres) corresponding to the number they received in the distribution of 2023.
This proximity principle aims to reﬂect a realistic scenario where the density of applicants
correlates with the number of high schools in the area, thereby simulating urban and suburban
population densities. A depiction of the high school and pupil addresses is given in ﬁgure 6.3
below.
==PAGE:64==
52
6.5. DATA GATHERING
Figure 6.3: The ﬁgure shows the density of the high schools (red pinpoints) and the pupil
addresses (blue dots).
Notice that not all pupil addresses are placed in residential areas. Restricting the addresses to
only residential zones has proven to be time-consuming. Therefore, random coordinates within
a speciﬁc residential, commercial or industrial area range are accepted as valid pupil addresses.
For more detail on how the addresses are generated, see the Python script in Appendix B.1.2
With both the pupil and high school addresses available, it is possible to utilize open-source
routing software to generate the shortest travel times for each applicant to each institution. To
achieve this, a local OTP server with updated GTFS routing data provided by Rejseplanen has
been created to compute the shortest travel time by public transport and walking, and a public
OSRM API is used to generate the shortest travel time by bicycle and car. These two routing
methods are then integrated into a Python script that computes the shortest travel time by
public transport, walking, or bike. When public transport is unavailable, the shortest time of
either biking or driving is used. The Python script computing the travel times is given in the
==PAGE:65==
6.6. PROBLEM SIZE AND MODEL SIMPLIFICATIONS
53
Appendix B.1.3.
Priorities, pij
Several principles are considered when generating realistic applicant priorities. The aim is to
mimic the results of the 2023 distribution detailed in section 6.5.1. The ﬁrst principle is as
follows:
The number of ﬁrst-priority applicants for each institution must not exceed the number they
received in the distribution of 2023.
This principle ensures that the distribution of the ﬁrst priorities of the applicants reﬂects that
of the 2023 distribution.
The second principle is about the percentage distribution across USE programs,
The overall percentage of pupils applying for each USE program does not exceed the percentage
distributed to each USE program in the 2023 distribution.
That is, if 50 % of all pupils received a spot at an STX high school in 2023, no more than 50 %
should apply for an STX high school in this case problem. This principle ensures a correlation
between the number of available spots and the number of applications.
The last principle follows the idea that the pupils prioritize the high schools within some perime-
ter of their homes.
The prioritized institutions of each pupil lie within 25 km of their homes
This principle is not based on the distribution of 2023, as there is no information on this
behaviour among the applicants; however, it is based on the crude assumption that most pupils
don’t want to travel too far daily.
Parental income levels, dkj
Each applicant is placed in one of the three income groups, and the aim is to reﬂect the depart-
ments’ income level distribution for 2020 presented in item 7) of section 6.5.1. The principle
applied is as follows:
The income level distribution of ﬁrst-priority applicants for an institution is designed such that
the proportion of applicants in each income level does not exceed the institution’s income level
distribution in 2020.
Each applicant is assigned an income level group based on their ﬁrst priority institution, ensuring
that the proportion of students in each group does not exceed the institution’s income level
distribution from 2020.
6.6
Problem size and model simpliﬁcations
The size of this thesis’s distribution model is not insigniﬁcant, as the number of variables and
constraints is large, consequently aﬀecting the MIP model’s computational complexity. It can,
therefore, be worthwhile to consider: 1) is it possible to reduce the problem size, and 2) will
any simpliﬁcations of the model aﬀect the overall goal of providing a proof-of-concept to the
proposed framework presented in section 5.1. This section will evaluate the problem size and
propose simpliﬁcations to the model that do not interact with the overall goal of the thesis. The
key metrics when assessing the size of the problem are given in table 6.4.
==PAGE:66==
54
6.6. PROBLEM SIZE AND MODEL SIMPLIFICATIONS
Set
Size
Description
I
16.621
The set contains all pupil applicants
J
85
The set contains the USE program departments of all institutions
S
60
The set contains all distinct institutions
K
3
Set of all income levels
H
5

Γ ⊆J
58
The subset of USE program departments that have parental income levels
Table 6.4: The table shows the size of the diﬀerent sets of the MIP model.
Given these metrics, the problem size can be calculated. First of all, the binary variables consist
of xij and yij for i ∈I and j ∈J , and δe
kj for k ∈K and j ∈J . The total number of binary
variables Nb is then calculated as follows,
Nb = 2 × |I| × |J | + |K| × |Γ|
= 2 × 16.621 × 85 + 3 × 58
= 2.825.744
Similarly, regarding the deviation variables δ+
kj and δ−
kj for k ∈K and j ∈Γ, the total number
of deviation variables Nd is given as
Nd = 2 × |K| × |Γ|
= 2 × 3 × 58
= 348
The total number of variables is NT = 2.825.744 + 348 = 2.826.092. Further, the total number
of constraints Nc of the model in (6.7a) is calculated as follows,
Nc = |I| + |I| + |J | + |S| + |H| + |I| × |J | + |I| + 3 × |K| × |Γ|
= 16.621 × (3 + 85) + 85 + 60 + 5 + 3 × 3 × 58
= 1.463.320
The problem size is substantial, with over 2.83 million variables and 1.46 million constraints,
highlighting the signiﬁcant computational complexity in solving this MIP model. Initial runs
of the model of section 6.4 have been conducted, solving each objective individually, and they
show signiﬁcantly slow convergence, especially considering the diversity objective, which was
not possible to solve within a reasonable time. Therefore, it is crucial to consider how to reduce
and simplify the model.
The ﬁrst point of interest is the high school situated at Bornholm. Even though it is a part
of the high schools of the capital region of Denmark, as seen in [49], a fair assumption is that
the 292 applicants from this area do not desire to travel 4 hours each way daily. Therefore,
they are pre-allocated to the one high school in Rønne and can be neglected in this analysis.
Similarly, it is assumed that no pupils apply to this institution if they do not live on Bornholm.
Subsequently, this high school, with its four USE programs, can also be neglected from the
analysis.
Further, the pupils applying for a proﬁle study line are practically pre-allocated to the speciﬁc
institutions oﬀering them. Due to their stationarity, it can be assumed without losing relevance
==PAGE:67==
6.6. PROBLEM SIZE AND MODEL SIMPLIFICATIONS
55
that no pupils are applying for proﬁle study lines. This means that the variables yij and the
constraints (6.7f) and (6.7g) can be removed from the model.
Another complication is the minimum capacity of each high school Qmin. Setting it to 84 makes
the problem infeasible, as some high schools do not have a total capacity of 84. To mitigate
this, Qmin is set to 58.
Additionally, introducing the binary auxiliary variable, δe
kj, complicates the model further. An
alternative approach that works in this case is to aggregate the constraints (6.7j) and (6.7k) into
one constraint that puts a bound on both variables simultaneously. That is,
δ+
kj + δ−
kj ≤Qj,
∀k ∈K, ∀j ∈Γ
(6.22)
The constraint ensures that the total deviation does not exceed the department’s capacity and
is substituted with the constraints (6.7j) and (6.7k).
The mentioned assumption reduces the model’s size signiﬁcantly. The total number of variables
is now,
NT = Nb + Nd
= (16.621 −291) × (85 −4) + 2 × 3 × 58
= 1.323.078
which is a reduction of 53.2%. Similarly, the total number of constraints is now,
Nc = |I| + |I| + |J | + |S| + |I| + |K| × |Γ|
= 3 × (16.621 −291) + (85 −4) + (60 −1) + 3 × 58
= 49.304
which is a reduction of 96.6%. These adjustments drastically reduce the model size and compu-
tational complexity.
However, solving the reduced MIP model as a single-objective problem with the diversity objec-
tive as the objective function still proved unattainable within a reasonable time frame. Therefore,
further simpliﬁcations are required regarding the constraint (6.7i).
The right-hand side of the constraint, (ajk
"
i∈I xij), reﬂects a variable target proportion that
involves considerable computational eﬀort to evaluate because the sum must be calculated dy-
namically as part of the optimization process. Replacing it with a constant makes the model
easier to handle computationally since the right-hand side will not change. An adequate al-
ternative is considering the capacity Qj of the USE program department j ∈Γ instead of the
summation term. That is,
ajk
!
i∈I
xij →ajkQj
(6.23)
In this way, the right-hand side becomes constant. While changing the right-hand side to a
constant can simplify the model and reduce computational complexity, it can also misrepresent
the actual problem. However, as the purpose of building and testing this model is to provide a
real-world problem acting as a proof-of-concept of the proposed framework, it is deemed a fair
simpliﬁcation of the problem.
The model size reduction and simpliﬁcations have drastically improved the computational run-
time, and the updated model is used in the subsequent analysis.

6.8
Generation of stakeholders
Given the complex nature of distributing pupils to upper secondary education programs in
Denmark, one can imagine various types of stakeholders involved in the decision-making process.
Beyond the model employed for this particular tasks, the decision-making process and ﬁnal choice
of solution is inherently of political nature. The selection of stakeholders and the composition
of the stakeholder groups are of great importance, as their preferences dictates the process and
process outcome. However, it is infeasible to include every stakeholder aﬀected by or interested
in the outcome. It is conceivable that the stakeholders involved in the actual decision-making
process to be representatives of private organisations, government entities and political parties,
all representing a broad spectrum of societal interests. Such stakeholders could be the following:
• The Government, represented by the minister of Children and Education.
• The opposition to the government, represented by one prominent politician from the
biggest opposition party.
• The president of the Danish National Union of Upper Secondary School Teachers (GL).
• The president of the Union of Danish Upper Secondary School Students (DGS).
Due to the inaccessibility of proposed stakeholders, scarce resources and time constraints, the
value proposition of the persona methodology becomes increasingly relevant in addressing these
challenges. Four personas are generated based broad empirical data and are succinctly presented
in table 6.8. To see the comprehensive descriptions of the personas, please refer to Appendix
A.2.

which have served as inspiration for creating the respective personas.
6.8.1
Elicitation of preferences
To elicit the preference proﬁles of the generated personas, a standardized questionnaire has
been designed, see appendix A.1. The questionnaire encompasses a series of questions aimed
at yielding information from which ELECTRE TRI parameters can be quantiﬁed. Moreover,
it includes potential alternatives that are to be compared by the personas. To achieve this,
the questionnaire predominantly utilizes a 5-point Likert scale [73] for ranking each question.
Each persona was imagined to respond to the questions based on their descriptions.
These
hypothetical answers have been carefully crafted to reﬂect the personas’ characteristics and
preferences. Although both the personas and their respective answers are ﬁctional, they are
intended to be treated as real entities, with responses grounded in realistic assumptions about
each persona’s behavior and preferences related to the problem.
==PAGE:73==
Chapter 7
Case Study Results
The problem of distributing high school students is deﬁned, and the mathematical optimization
model and the preference proﬁles of the generated stakeholders are established in the previous
chapter. Subsequently, the results of applying the proposed framework to the case are presented
in this chapter and represent steps 5 to 7 of the proposed framework in ﬁgure 5.1, along with one
feedback loop (step 7a) to simulate the framework’s interactive aspect and ﬂexibility. Therefore,
two iterations of the NEIMO algorithm will be evaluated, and the preference proﬁles and model
constraints will be updated in the second iteration.
The ﬁrst section will present the approximated set of well-diversiﬁed, non-dominated Pareto
optimal solutions to estimate the nadir point. The following section will show and explain the
results of running the NEIMO algorithm on the case presented.
7.1
Estimation of Pareto front
The Pareto front has been estimated using the TPA algorithm [15] found within the Julia
JuMP package ’MultiObjectiveOptimization.jl’ [74], which is a recent extension to the JuMP
optimization toolbox. This algorithm can ﬁnd exact and well-diversiﬁed Pareto optimal solutions
even though it is run with a time limit. This property is of utmost importance in this case since
it is used to estimate the nadir point, and as explained in section 3.2.7, this task requires a set
of well-spread solutions of the entire Pareto front to come up with a reasonable estimation of
the nadir point. The MIP has been set up in Julia, and the TPA algorithm has yielded the
non-dominated solutions illustrated in ﬁgure 7.1a. The code is provided in Appendix B.7.
==PAGE:74==
62
7.1. ESTIMATION OF PARETO FRONT
(a) Approximated Pareto front 3D
(b) Priority/Transport
(c) Transport/Diversity
(d) Priority/Diversity
Figure 7.1: The ﬁgure shows the approximated Pareto front from diﬀerent perspectives both as
3D and 2D projections.
Figure 7.1a illustrates the approximated Pareto front solutions (red dots), along with the nadir
objective vector znad (green dot) and the ideal objective vector z∗(blue dot). Figure 7.1b-7.1d
shows 2D projections of the Pareto front from all dimensions. The ideal point (blue dot) and
the nadir point (green dot) are also included.
The Julia program generated 1868 exact solutions within a timeframe of 43 hours. The speciﬁc
values of the estimated nadir and ideal points are,

The 3D plot in Figure 7.1a illustrates that the solutions span a broad area of the Pareto front,
which is expected given the anytime algorithm employed. However, it is also apparent that
many solutions are clustered along a line with explicitly high diversity objective values. This
behaviour can be attributed to the nature of the model’s constraints. Speciﬁcally, the diversity
variables are only limited by the departments’ capacity, which, for high values of the diversity
objective, allows for numerous combinations that satisfy the constraints related to the priority
and transportation time objectives. Another possible explanation could be the search method
of the algorithm, but exploring this further is beyond the scope of this thesis.
==PAGE:75==
7.2. NEIMO ALGORITHM RESULTS
63
The 2D projection in ﬁgure 7.1b shows a downward-sloping curve illustrating a clear trade-oﬀ
relation between the priority and transportation time objectives. As one objective improves, the
other objective tends to worsen. Although the diversity objective is not directly visible in the
ﬁgure, the solutions do not vary much from this curve, indicating that the diversity objective
does not heavily impact the trade-oﬀrelations. This statement is further supported by the 2D
projections in ﬁgures 7.1c and 7.1d. In ﬁgure 7.1c, solutions can be found very close to the ideal
objective point (bottom left corner), and in ﬁgure 7.1d, they can be found even closer. These
projections indicate that the ideal objective value corresponding to the diversity objective can
almost be obtained without compromising the values of the other objectives, i.e., the diversity
objective does not conﬂict with the priority and transportation time objectives.
7.2
NEIMO algorithm Results
Two iterations of the NEIMO algorithm have been simulated to test the proposed framework.
For both iterations, the technical parameters presented in Appendix A.3 have been used, and
the scalarizing functions of the algorithm have been run in parallel on a MacBook Pro M1 Max
2021 with ten available cores.
In the ﬁrst iteration of the algorithm, the four stakeholder preference proﬁles presented in
Appendix A.2 are utilized. The algorithm terminates when N=20 solutions in category c3 are
identiﬁed for each stakeholder.

other stakeholders. As with the analysis of the entire Pareto front, this observation supports
the indication of the diversity criterion being non-conﬂicting.
==PAGE:82==
Chapter 8
Discussion
This chapter will examine the diﬀerent aspects of the proposed framework and the case study.
The initial section will address the uncertainties related to the case study and assess how ac-
curately the model and stakeholders represent the real world. Then, a sensitivity analysis will
investigate the inﬂuence of diﬀerent model parameters on the outcome. The subsequent sections
will discuss the methodology choices and reﬂect upon possible alternative approaches. Lastly,
general reﬂections on the proposed framework, its usage, and its implications will be presented.
8.1
Case Study Uncertainties
A lot of inherent uncertainty is present in relation to the case study conducted in this master’s
thesis.
First of all, there is minimal availability of necessary data.
As discussed in section
6.5, the highly sensitive personal information of the applicants required in the mathematical
model might conﬂict with GDPR. More eﬀorts could have been put into obtaining accurate
data, requiring correspondence with the Ministry of Education and potentially having to work
on secured servers. However, early in the project, it was decided to generate the inaccessible
data to be as realistic as possible. The extent to which this is achieved is debatable. Due to
time constraints, the student addresses have been created not just in residential areas but in
industrial and commercial areas, resulting in student addresses in the Baltic Sea or other areas
inaccessible by public transport or bike. These addresses signiﬁcantly impact transportation
time, as they are based on GTFS routing data and OSRM API, and the fastest transportation
mode in these cases is possibly by foot. It can, therefore, result in overestimating the students’
travel time.
Further, the priority choices of each student are highly subjective, and diﬀerent things like travel
time, choices of close friends, the reputation of the schools, etc., aﬀect the choice. Due to the
diﬃculties in obtaining these priority structures, it was decided that the priorities and the income
level distribution are estimated based on previous distributions. Assigning priorities, addresses
and income level groups to the students based on last distribution data can be considered
a knapsack problem that could be solved using a linear program. However, this is beyond the
scope of the thesis, and instead, these parameters have been assigned purely heuristically guided
by the principles explained in section 6.5.2.
This thesis introduces multiple stakeholder personas to simulate diﬀerent opinions and view-
points on the distribution problem and to illustrate how these diﬀerent viewpoints can be in-
corporated into a framework to guide the search for desired alternatives. Their opinions and
perspectives are built on already-expressed public opinions as described in 6.8 and support the
elicitation of preference proﬁles by simulating how they would answer a questionnaire. Even
==PAGE:83==
8.1. CASE STUDY UNCERTAINTIES
71
though they are purely ﬁctional, they can help put context into diﬀerent choices, and in real-
life cases with scarce resources and time constraints, using the persona methodology to reﬂect
absent stakeholders’ opinions could be beneﬁcial.
Generating value functions requires interaction with DMs to ensure that the functions reﬂect
their preferences for diﬀerent levels of achievement on a measurable scale [6]. In this case study,
diﬀerent modelling choices were made to deﬁne these functions. Considering the discontinuous
nature of prioritizing departments, the priority value function was designed by directly assigning
a score from 0 to 100 to each priority value. For transportation time and diversity, scores from 0
to 100 were assigned to diﬀerent reference points, and logistic regression was used to deﬁne the
value functions. Alternatively, the bisection method or the diﬀerence method could have been
applied. These indirect methods are widely used and are constructed to guide the DM intuitively
in assigning scores to reference points [6]. Both methods require well-deﬁned endpoints but
diﬀer in their approach. The bisection method iteratively narrows down the value function by
having the decision-maker compare midpoint values within a range until a satisfactory set of
points is deﬁned. Generally, ﬁve points are accepted (two endpoints and three midpoints) [6].
The diﬀerence method involves the decision-maker ranking increments in criterion values on
an objectively measured scale to determine the relative increase in value, thereby shaping the
value function [6]. You can argue that using one of these methods would be a better approach
as it can be diﬃcult to directly elicit scores to some chosen reference points, as done in this
case study. Another consideration when determining the value functions of the criteria is what
objective values can be obtained. The initial approach to deﬁning the reference points of the
transportation value function was to consider the intervals 0-14 min, 15-29 min, 30-44 min, and
45+ min of the distribution results in 2023 [49] and assign a score to each start points of these
intervals. That is, [0,100],[15,100],[30,20], and [45,0]. However, solving the MIP model with the
transportation time as the only objective yielded a low transport value score, indicating that the
initial reference points are not scaled properly to what can be achieved by the model. Therefore,
the value function was changed to the one presented in Figure 6.2 to ﬁt better.
Considering the realism of the case study results, an intuitive approach is to compare them
with the 2023 distribution results [49]. From this report, it can be deduced that the average
student across all USE programs received their ﬁrst priority, with an average travel time of
around 28 minutes. However, when examining the solutions in Figure 7.2, it is evident that
such a solution is not possible. In one of the solutions, where the average student is assigned
to their ﬁrst priority department, the average travel time is 51 minutes. Since this solution is
Pareto optimal, no other solution with the same priority score but a lower travel time exists.
Therefore, not many insights into the actual distribution are gained.
Nevertheless, the case study successfully demonstrates that priority and transportation time
are two conﬂicting criteria, which is realistic given the general public’s ongoing interest in the
problem of distributing high school students. As indicated in chapter 7, both the analysis of
the ideal point on the estimated Pareto front in Figures 7.1a-7.1d and the variation analysis
illustrations in Figures 7.3 and 7.5 show that the diversity criterion does not necessarily conﬂict
with the other criteria.
While this non-conﬂicting criterion does not cause the problem to
be infeasible, it suggests that including it as an objective in the model may be redundant. An
alternative approach is to incorporate the desire to minimize deviation from income level targets
into the objective functions of the remaining objectives as an added penalty term, normalized to
match the speciﬁc objectives. This reduces the dimensionality and complexity of the problem.
Diﬀerentiating between conﬂicting and non-conﬂicting criteria in real life is not trivial (and
one might argue whether mutually exclusive and conﬂicting criteria even exist), so it may be
beneﬁcial to perform an initial analysis of the objectives, as done in this case study, to identify
==PAGE:84==
72
8.2. SENSITIVITY ANALYSIS
non-conﬂicting criteria. Even though the insights gained from this case study are limited, it
demonstrates a diﬀerent and more holistic angle to tackling the problem of distributing high
school students compared to the currently implemented approach. It considers trade-oﬀs between
priority, transportation time, and diversity criteria and utilizes stakeholder preferences instead
of looking at each criterion lexicographically. This enables an analysis of the interdependence
of the criteria and raises the question of whether the diversity criterion is redundant in reality
or if measuring diversity by looking at income-level groups is appropriate.
8.2
Sensitivity Analysis
As stated on multiple occasions throughout this thesis, there are many parameters to be de-
termined both in connection with the elicitation of preference proﬁles of the stakeholders (e.g.,
proﬁle, preference, indiﬀerence, and veto thresholds, as well as the λ-cutting level) and as part
of the NEIMO algorithm (e.g., the probability ξ deciding if the algorithm should explore new or
exploit existing solutions, the increase and decrease factors FI,FD, classiﬁcation thresholds TM,
TI, and augmentation coeﬃcient ρ). An extensive sensitivity analysis to determine the impact of
diﬀerent parameters on the results and identify which are most inﬂuential on the overall system
is not conducted in this thesis, as it would require more resources than available. However,
a subset of the parameters are investigated to illustrate their impact on the resulting Pareto
optimal solutions. These parameters are the λ-cutting level, the proﬁle thresholds b1 and b2,
and the probability ξ.
As randomness is integrated into the NEIMO algorithms search process, the sensitivity analysis
is based on the average results of ﬁve algorithm runs, each stopping after 100 iterations. All tests
have been conducted using the preference proﬁles and parameters outlined in Appendix A.2 and
Table A.1, respectively, with variations speciﬁc to each test. The Pareto front visualization of
all tests only shows one out of the ﬁve runs. A reference test with the standard parameters
has been established, and the corresponding measures are shown in Table 8.1. The result is
presented in Figure 8.1.
Figure 8.1: The ﬁgure shows one of the ﬁve benchmark runs. The parameters correspond to
Table A.1 and ﬁgure A.1 in Appendix A.

The ﬁrst sensitivity test is conducted for diﬀerent λ-cutting levels. This threshold determines the
minimum credibility required for an alternative to be assigned to a speciﬁc category. Therefore,
increasing this value is expected to result in fewer category c3 solutions. Usually, this value is
set between 0.5 and 1 depending on the conﬁdence level required in the decision process. The
benchmark test has λ = 0.75, so the two other tested values are λ = 0.5 and λ = 1. The results
are seen in Figure 8.2 and Table 8.2.

The last parameter to investigate is the explore probability ξ that is used in the NEIMO algo-
rithm to determine whether to explore new solutions with a random reference point from the
approximated Pareto front or if it should exploit the objective space near recent solutions (see
section 5.2 for more information of the algorithm). Decreasing this probability will emphasize
exploitation, and therefore, the solutions are expected to be more clustered. Conversely, increas-
ing the probability value will result in more diverse solutions. The sensitivity analysis for this
==PAGE:87==
8.2. SENSITIVITY ANALYSIS
75
parameter is conducted with three diﬀerent probability values, ξ = 0.5, ξ = 0.1 and ξ = 0.05.
