Visual inspection of Figure 8.4a, where there is a 50% chance of exploring, shows that the
solutions are generally well-spread across the solution space, with no clear clusters forming.
However, when comparing these solutions with those in Figures 8.1, 8.4b, and 8.4c, it is evident
that the solutions gradually become more clustered and larger areas of the solution space remain
unexplored. Furthermore, the box plots in Figure 8.4d indicate a central tendency: as the ξ-
probability decreases, the solutions condense around c3 solutions. This is observed through the
median distance from each point to the nearest c3 solution, which gradually decreases. Inter-
estingly, this tendency does not necessarily result in a higher number of c3 solutions, as demon-
strated in Table 8.4. The number of c3 solutions on average increases when the ξ-probability
decreases from 0.5 to 0.25 by 16 and further increases from 0.25 to 0.10 by 8. However, decreas-
ing the probability to 0.05 results in an average decrease of 11 c3 solutions. This suggests that
the optimal value of ξ lies between 0.1 and 0.05.
It is noteworthy to keep in mind that the exploration step depends on the solutions of the
approximated Pareto front and how diverse they are. Considering that a cluster of the solutions
shown in Figure 7.1 exists along a line where the deviation values are high, having a high
ξ-probability will likely result in a high density of solutions near this line. This tendency is
reﬂected in the solutions in Figure 8.4a. To mitigate this skewness in exploring the objective
space, a subset of well-diverse solutions from the approximated Pareto front can be used as
reference points in the exploration step of the NEIMO algorithm.
Visual inspection of the spread of the solutions is only possible in cases with three or fewer
criteria, and measuring the Euclidean distance to c3 solutions might not provide the best mea-
sure for determining how clustered the solutions are. For example, in the case of a large dense
cluster of c1 solutions with a great distance to the nearest c3 solution, the measured distance
would be large, even though the solutions are clearly clustered. Therefore, Alternative per-
formance measures are needed to thoroughly investigate the inﬂuence of the ξ-probability on
how clustered the solutions are. An approach is measuring the distance from each point to the
nearest neighbouring point. If the distances are generally large, it could indicate that the solu-
tions are well-diverse and vice versa. Another approach could be to consider diﬀerent clustering
algorithms and evaluate how clustered the solutions are.
Nevertheless, the sensitivity analysis has shown that the NEIMO algorithm behaves as expected
when the chosen parameters are varied. As mentioned, the algorithm contains many parameters,
emphasizing the need for an extensive sensitivity analysis when implementing the framework.
The analysis shows that running the algorithm for 100 iterations results in an average runtime
of around 2 hours for all tests and is therefore not inﬂuenced by the changing parameter values.
Further, special attention should be given to determining the probability ξ, as the trade-oﬀ
between exploration and exploitation inﬂuences the computational cost and the convergence
quality.
==PAGE:89==
8.3. DISCUSSION OF CHOSEN METHODS
77
8.3
Discussion of Chosen Methods
The framework employs a hybrid MCDM and MOP approach, embedding the principles of
ELECTRE TRI into the synchronous NIMBUS method to guide the solution process towards
regions of interest and identify a preferred subset of solutions according to the preferences of
the DM. The guidance and categorization of solutions are the merits of the ELECTRE TRI pa-
rameters and outranking indices. However, the touchpoints between the Synchronous NIMBUS
method and the DMs are essentially replaced by the ELECTRE TRI parameters. This makes the
framework theoretically and practically less interactive, as DMs are less involved in the solution
process, potentially reducing the learning aspect - one of the key advantages of the NIMBUS
method. However, the framework maintains some touchpoints, for instance, when the DMs are
presented with the ﬁndings of the solutions contained within the best category, and the DMs
can update their preferences if needed. However, the interplay between ELECTRE TRI and the
synchronous NIMBUS can potentially oﬀset the cognitive burden of frequent touchpoints. Part
of this interplay can be distinguished as a sorting problem, explicitly referring to the categoriza-
tion of solutions by ELECTRE TRI. In this case, a method addressing the sorting problem is
favourable compared to the other types of problematics mentioned in section 4.2, consequently
delimiting choices of MCDM approaches. The choice of ranking approach is inherently based on
relative judgements, making the evaluations entirely dependent on the alternatives considered.
For instance, employing a choice problematic approach, such as ELECTRE I instead of ELEC-
TRE TRI, would yield a local outranking relation between the four subproblem solutions for a
particular iteration of the NEIMO algorithm. However, the best-performing alternative might
be wrongfully saved to the set of preferred solutions, as that speciﬁc alternative might conﬂict
with the global preferences of the DM(s).
ELECTRE TRI is a well-known and widely used method in relation to the sorting problem,
even though the preference elicitation process and the determination of parameters can be
challenging. This challenge is only ampliﬁed when considering MaOPs. In the case presented
in the thesis, only three objectives were considered. The DMs, consisting of the stakeholder
group presented in section 6.8, were also assumed to have a sound understanding of the solution
space of the decision problem. Similar assumptions may not apply to other decision problems.
Therefore, the cognitive burden imposed on the DM by specifying their initial preferences a
priori in the framework might be far more signiﬁcant than distributing high school students.
However, the interactive nature of the framework’s feedback loop allows the DMs to learn about
the decision problem as the solution progresses, similarly to the synchronous NIMBUS method,
alleviating the cognitive burden to a certain extent. Alternatively, the ﬁrst iterations of the
NEIMO algorithm can be executed with some proxy parameters, e.g., a cutting level of 0.5,
similar indiﬀerence, preference and veto threshold, and all objectives are considered to be equally
important; after that, the relevant parameters are decided upon.
Central to the synchronous NIMBUS is estimating the nadir point. In the high school distribu-
tion problem of this thesis, the nadir point is determined from an approximation of the entire
Pareto front, which is constituted by 1868 exact Pareto optimal points, found by the anytime
algorithm described in section 3.2.4, with a signiﬁcant runtime of 43 hours. However, the ap-
proach of ﬁrst estimating the entire Pareto optimal set, either by the anytime algorithm or an
MOEA, and then determining the nadir point from the set seems to cause a paradox. The main
reason for ﬁnding the nadir point is to use it in interactive methods to ﬁnd Pareto optimal
solutions. However, suppose the entire Pareto front is already estimated. In that case, it raises
the question of whether it would be better to utilize the entire estimated Pareto front instead of
the proposed approach and then apply the ELECTRE TRI method a posteriori on the identiﬁed
solutions. The obtained Pareto front could be classiﬁed into pre-deﬁned categories, allowing the
==PAGE:90==
78
8.4. FRAMEWORK REFLECTIONS
DM to restrict the choice of solutions according to their preferences. This alternative necessitates
a robust preference system to be in place and can be computationally heavy, especially if the
number of objectives increases signiﬁcantly. It also results in a signiﬁcant number of solutions
being discarded, as only a small subset would be of interest to the DM. Nevertheless, it might
still be more eﬃcient than ﬁrst using signiﬁcant resources to estimate the nadir point followed by
the NEIMO algorithm utilizing the synchronous NIMBUS and ELECTRE TRI. Subsequently,
for the proposed framework to be truly eﬃcient, it requires estimating the nadir point quickly.
One of NIMBUS’s strong points is its interactive approach, so similar philosophies have been
explored about MOEAs. Here, the DM’s preferences are embedded into the MOEA, guiding
the solution process towards areas of interest and minimizing the focus on intermediate solu-
tions irrelevant to the DM. More speciﬁcally, the choices of main operators of the MOEA (e.g.,
mutation and selection) are iteratively assessed by the DM to ensure the iterations reﬂect their
preferences. In general, the mechanics of MOEAs are more transparent than the commercial
solvers used by NIMBUS, as the operations within commercial solvers are black-boxed. There-
fore, integrating ELECTRE-TRI into an MOEA can make sense as it oﬀers greater ﬂexibility in
the search process and generates more solutions within the same period.
Additionally, estimating the nadir point is challenging, for which state-of-the-art literature sug-
gests a modiﬁed MOEA. However, MOEAs are typically case-speciﬁc and do not leverage power-
ful commercial exact solvers and packages, such as JuMP, Gurobi, and CPLEX, as the NEIMO
algorithm can. This is particularly relevant given the recent advancements in anytime algo-
rithms, which can ﬁnd well-diversiﬁed solutions regardless of time constraints, e.g., the TPA
algorithm introduced with the JuMP package ’MultiObjectiveOptimization.jl’ in March 2023.
These anytime algorithms provide a signiﬁcant advantage in estimating the nadir point and a
driving factor of the eﬃciency of the proposed framework.
8.4
Framework Reﬂections
Despite numerous reports on hybridizing MCDM and MOP methods and the extensive literature
on interactive methods, there is a lack of techniques that can provide performance measures on
how well the approach satisﬁes the needs of the DM [14], [16]. This gap makes it challenging
to assess the proposed framework concerning existing methods outlined in the literature review.
Without objective performance measures, evaluations heavily rely on subjective criteria, which
can be diﬀerent among DMs and may not accurately reﬂect the robustness or eﬃciency of the
approach. Furthermore, the reliance on DM’s satisfaction as a termination criterion for many
approaches raises concerns about the consistency and reproducibility of results. If DMs have
diﬀerent levels of understanding of the problem or expertise with a particular approach, their
satisfaction may not be a reliable performance indicator. Assessing the proposed framework
with human DMs is crucial for verifying its practicability. The overall satisfaction of the DMs
is essential, as it determines whether the proposed framework can be widely accepted and used
in practice. While it is possible to test the ﬁnal solution the DMs choose, other crucial factors
such as the preference elicitation process, the number of touchpoints and interaction interfaces,
and the information presented to them are also important. Therefore, it is recommended that
standardized measures for these techniques be developed to ensure fair comparisons between
diﬀerent approaches.
The type of MOP the proposed framework can address is determined by the method used to
approximate the nadir point and the commercial solver used by the NEIMO algorithm. For
the high school distribution problem, TPA, as described in section 3.2.4, was used to estimate
the nadir point, thus eliminating the need for deﬁning an MOEA for this purpose. The NIM-
BUS method is versatile and can handle linear and nonlinear problems involving continuous and
==PAGE:91==
8.4. FRAMEWORK REFLECTIONS
79
integer-valued variables; therefore, if the analyst can deﬁne a mathematical model representing
the decision problem, the proposed framework can be utilized to identify a desired solution. This
versatility makes the proposed framework general in nature, enabling it to address various real-
life problems. Furthermore, using MIP models in Julia allows ﬂexibility in solver use, especially
if new and better solvers are introduced, allowing the proposed framework to leverage these ad-
vancements. The high school distribution problem can be considered a signiﬁcant combinatorial
problem, with approximately 1.3 million variables and 49,000 constraints after simpliﬁcations.
It might have been favourable to employ either an MOEA to approximate the nadir point or an
interactive MOEA approach to ﬁnd a preferred solution, as they are frequently used and eﬃcient.
However, the computational complexity increases signiﬁcantly in tandem with the number of
objectives considered, and it has been demonstrated that the searchability of Pareto dominance
methods, such as NSGA-II, degrades as the number of objectives increases[75]. This degradation
might not be an issue for the NEIMO algorithm, as it solves single-objective problems and thus
can leverage powerful and well-established solvers.
==PAGE:92==
Chapter 9
Conclusion and Future Work
Historically, the MOP and MCDM communities have evolved separately. However, a newfound
interest in the synergies between the two ﬁelds has emerged in recent years, leading to a promis-
ing research ﬁeld that leverages the combined strengths of both ﬁelds while mitigating their
weaknesses [4] in addressing MaOPs. This synergy is particularly beneﬁcial, as many real-life
optimization problems often involve multiple objectives and multiple stakeholders.
In section 3.3, hybridized MCDM and MOP approaches were outlined. The literature study
revealed that many hybridized approaches were grounded in MOEAs in combination with an
MCDM approach. Various approaches have been proposed to deal with optimization problems
either a priori, a posteriori, interactively or in combination. With today’s increased computa-
tional capabilities and the growing complexity of data, the combination of MCDM approaches
and MOP methods in a combined framework appears promising in enhancing the decision-
making process in real-life problems. However, four areas of concern were identiﬁed when de-
veloping a hybridized framework. (a) Scalability. (b) Involvement of multiple stakeholders. (c)
Reducing the burden of decision-makers. (d) Performance assessment of hybridized MCDM and
MOP methods.
In this master’s thesis, a step-by-step decision-making framework is proposed to assist decision-
makers in eliciting preferences and embedding them into a guided search for preferred solutions
facilitated by a novel algorithm, NIMBUS-ELECTRE Integrated Multi-Objective (NEIMO) al-
gorithm. The proposed framework follows an 8-step procedure. First, the problem and criteria
are deﬁned. Next, a Mixed-Integer Programming Model is built. After, the Pareto front is
approximated to obtain an estimation of the nadir point by a method of choice; in this case, the
state-of-the-art anytime algorithm, TPA, was employed. Following this, ELECTRE TRI param-
eters are chosen based on the decision makers’ preferences. The NEIMO algorithm is executed
in the ﬁfth step, and the results are presented to the decision-makers. If the decision-makers
are satisﬁed with the solutions, the most preferred is selected, and the framework concludes.
However, if the decision-makers are dissatisﬁed, the ELECTRE TRI parameters are updated
accordingly, potential model constraints are added, and the NEIMO algorithm is executed again.
This feedback loop continues until the decision-makers are satisﬁed.
The proposed framework was applied to a signiﬁcant real-life combinatorial multi-objective op-
timization problem. After necessary simpliﬁcations, the high school distribution problem en-
compassed approximately 1.3 million variables and 49,000 constraints, respectively, with three
objectives to be minimized simultaneously: priority, transportation time, and diversity. Due to
the sensitive nature of the chosen optimization problem and time constraints, it was deemed
==PAGE:93==
81
necessary to generate artiﬁcial data based on realistic assumptions and previous distributions of
pupils. Moreover, the decision-makers constituted by the stakeholder group were generated by
the design-thinking method, personas. Each of the four personas was based on empirical data
and informed assumptions to represent the potential people involved in the decision-making pro-
cess accurately. The four personas were used to determine the ELECTRE TRI parameters and
represent conﬂicting perspectives on the high school distribution problem. Two iterations of the
NEIMO algorithm were simulated to test the proposed framework. The ﬁrst iteration aimed to
provide a diverse range of potential preferred solutions, allowing the stakeholders to understand
how their preference proﬁles inﬂuence the solution space. The second iteration aimed to illustrate
the feedback mechanism in the proposed framework and how altering preference parameters and
the addition of constraints aﬀect the solution space. As expected, the second iteration resulted
in a more dense solution space, fewer preferred solutions and a considerably longer running
time. Furthermore, the simulation revealed considerable evidence that the diversity objective
does not conﬂict with other objectives. This could result from the data estimation, indicating
that diversity is genuinely non-conﬂicting in the real-world scenario or a combination of both.
Nevertheless, the data generation makes the case study results susceptible to uncertainty and
challenging to compare with the actual previous distributions of high school pupils.
The sensitivity analysis conﬁrms that the NEIMO algorithm performs as expected with vary-
ing parameters. Given the numerous parameters involved, such as the probability ξ, proﬁle
thresholds, and λ-cutting levels, it is crucial to conduct an extensive sensitivity analysis in
real-life applications. The study also reveals that the average runtime remains consistent at
approximately 2 hours for 100 iterations, regardless of parameter changes. Lastly, the analysis
shows that emphasis should be put on determining the probability ξ, as the balance between
exploration and exploitation signiﬁcantly impacts the computational cost and the convergence
quality.
The implication of the results and sensitivity analysis eﬀectively shows that the proposed frame-
work can address the areas of concern to some extent. (a) is mitigated by using the synchronous
NIMBUS method, as the multi-objective problem is transformed into a single-objective problem
that Gurobi eﬀectively solves. However, approximating the Pareto front as a step to estimate
the nadir point becomes increasingly diﬃcult as the number of objectives rises. This is pertinent
to many approaches addressing MaOPs. However, speciﬁc algorithms such as the extremized
crowded NSGA-II have been developed to estimate the nadir point for MaOPs quickly. To deal
with (b), the proposed framework introduces multiple stakeholders with diﬀerent perspectives
to shed light on the various solutions obtained based on the decision makers’ preferences and
to uncover if groups of stakeholders share common Pareto optimal solutions. Nevertheless, this
remains a complex area to resolve, as a single individual ultimately makes the ﬁnal decision.
(c) is addressed, as the proposed framework maintains the constructive element from the inter-
active method in a feedback loop, allowing the decision-makers to learn about the problem as
the solution process progresses. Furthermore, the NEIMO algorithm alleviates the burden of
frequent touchpoints between the decision-makers and the interactive method. However, many
parameters still need to be determined, which can be challenging. Additionally, the complexity
increases as the number of objectives grows. The proposed framework cannot address (d), as no
human decision-makers were involved in the solution process. Despite actual decision makers
being involved, assessing the performance inherently becomes more challenging, as the frame-
work’s termination criteria rely on the decision makers’ subjective opinions. This limitation
highlights the need for further research involving real decision-makers to assess the validity of
the proposed framework.
In the proposed framework, Synchronous NIMBUS and ELECTRE TRI, two widely known
==PAGE:94==
82
9.1. FUTURE WORK
and applied methods, were combined to leverage their respective advantages. The former is
adept at ﬁnding exact Pareto optimal solutions, while the latter can eﬃciently categorize a
set of solutions. Central to both of the methods are the preferences of the decision-maker. A
limited amount of hybrid MCDM and MOP approaches exist in the literature, focusing on exact
methods, especially the combination of the previously mentioned methods. Even though some
of the critical areas identiﬁed are left unresolved, the proposed framework still contributes to
the promising research ﬁeld for hybridized MOP and MCDM methods addressing MaOPs with
multiple stakeholders.
9.1
Future Work
Further work is needed to assess the proposed framework’s viability in a more general context.
This could potentially entice two things. First, it is essential to include actual stakeholders in
the solution process to address the decision problem, as in the case study or a similar decision
problem, and assess their satisfaction after a preferred solution is identiﬁed. Second, developing
a standardized performance or evaluation measure for hybrid MCDM and MOP methods would
allow for the assessment of the proposed framework itself and a fair comparison of its performance
against alternative approaches proposed in the literature.
The elicitation process of the required parameters should be explored further to reduce the
complexity and cognitive burden of the decision-makers, especially when addressing MaOPs.
An option would be to reduce the objective dimensionality, e.g., a pre-screening of whether
the chosen objectives are conﬂicting before the elicitation process begins, as an objective is
potentially redundant if it correlates with some of the other objectives. A viable method would
be the principal component analysis (PCA).
Furthermore, exploring alternatives to the ELECTRE TRI method for categorizing alternatives
could be beneﬁcial.
One such alternative is the PROMSORT method [76].
Similar to the
ELECTRE TRI method, PROMSORT also pre-deﬁnes categories to which the alternatives are
assigned. However, the mechanics of the assignment procedure diﬀers, as PROMSORT uses a
reference set of alternatives during the procedure and could, in some ways, be more transparent
and easier to understand than the ELECTRE TRI method [76].
Another aspect of the proposed framework that is noteworthy for exploring would be the eﬀect
of changing the increase and decrease factors and their implications for the exploration and
exploitation step. If, after each iteration, the aspiration levels were only slightly increased or
decreased, one could assume that the following solutions would be in close proximity to the
current solution. Conversely, if the aspirations were signiﬁcantly increased or decreased, one
could assume that the solutions would be more distant from the current solution.

Appendix A
Case Study
A.1
Questionaire for Stakeholder Preference Elicitation
==PAGE:102==
Eliciting preference parameters. 
 
Prior to the eliciting of preference parameters, all involved stakeholders will get a thorough 
briefing on how the different objectives, criteria, and input for ELECTRE TRI are defined, to 
create a common language between analysts and stakeholders. Therefore, the stakeholders 
will participate in a workshop prior to answering the respective questions.  
 
Weights of the different criteria:  
The weight eliciting process is comprised of the following questions, which will guide the 
stakeholder and the analyst to create sound arguments for the different criteria weights 
specific to the persona.  
 
Priority:  
• How important is it for pupils to be placed in their 1st priority choice compared to 
ensuring short transportation times and considering parental income? 
o Not important. 
o Slightly important. 
o Moderately important. 
o Very important. 
o Extremely important. 
• If a trade-off is necessary, would you prioritize allocating pupils to their 1st priority or 
ensuring shorter transportation times? 
o Prioritize 1st priority allocation. 
o Prioritize shorter transportation times. 
o Consider both equally. 
• How would you feel about a situation where most pupils do not get their 1st priority 
but have shorter transportation times? 
o Very dissatisfied. 
o Dissatisfied. 
o Neutral. 
o Satisfied. 
o Very satisfied. 
 
Transportation time: 
Significance of transportation time: 
• How important is minimizing transportation time for pupils compared to having a 
sustainable composition of pupils based on parental income and 1st priority 
allocation? 
o Not important. 
o Slightly important. 
o Moderately important. 
o Very important. 
o Extremely important. 
• To what degree do you agree with the following statement: I prefer a scenario where 
pupils spend more time commuting if it means they can attend their 1st priority? 
o Strongly disagree. 
o Disagree. 
==PAGE:103==
o Neutral. 
o Agree 
o Strongly agree. 
How would you rate the importance of having schools within a close proximity to pupils’ 
homes? 
o Slightly important. 
o Moderately important. 
o Very important. 
o Extremely important. 
• If a trade-off is necessary, would you prioritize or ensuring shorter transportation 
times or a progressive composition of pupils at the highs schools? 
o Prioritize shorter transportation times. 
o Ensure 
o Consider both equally. 
 
Parental income: 
• How important is it to have a diverse composition of students at schools based on 
parental income compared to priority and transportation times? 
o Not important. 
o Slightly important. 
o Moderately important. 
o Very important. 
o Extremely important. 
• How should parental income influence the capacity for each school? 
o No influence 
o Minor influence 
o Moderate influence 
o Major influence 
o Primary influence 
 
 
Categories and profiles for each criterion:  
You are presented with the respective value functions for each criterion / objective. Your 
task is to segment the value function into three categories: Good, medium, bad. This is 
effectively done by specifying two vertical lines. The two vertical lines determines the 
‘profile’ values for each criterion.  
 
Thresholds:  
To help the stakeholder define the respective thresholds, the stakeholder is presented with 
‘potential’ alternatives, and are asked how they compare to the profiles they defined 
previously. The stakeholders are made to merely compare the presented alternatives or 
scenarios, and the analyst will then quantify the values accordingly.

# Calculate
distance
between
two points on the
surface of the Earth
39
function
distance(start , stop)
40
lat1 = start [1]
41
lon1 = start [2]
42
lat2 = stop [1]
43
lon2 = stop [2]
44
45
r = 6371 # Earth ’s radius in km
46
47
# Convert
degrees to radians
48
rlat1 = deg2rad(lat1)
49
rlon1 = deg2rad(lon1)
50
rlat2 = deg2rad(lat2)
51
rlon2 = deg2rad(lon2)
52
53
# Calculate
the great -circle
distance
between
two points on the
surface of
the Earth
54
dis = acos(sin(rlat1) * sin(rlat2) + cos(rlat1) * cos(rlat2) * (cos(rlon2 -
rlon1))) * r
55
return dis
56
end

function
concordance_i (a, b, qj , pj)
6
c = 0 # Initializing
the

function
discordance_i (a, b, vj , pj)
27
d = 0 # Initializing
the
discordance
index
28
if a < b - vj
29
d = 1
30
elseif a >= b - pj
31
d = 0
32

33

function
credibility(aj , bj , q, p, w, v)
39
# if d_concordance
index is 1, then
credibility
index is 0.
40
ci = 0
41
dis_vector = zeros(length(p))
42
cc = compConcord(aj , bj , q, p, w)
43
for n in eachindex(p)
44
dis_vector[n] = discordance_i (aj[n], bj[n], v[n], p[n])
45
end
46
if 1 in dis_vector
47
ci = 0
48
elseif cc
>= maximum(dis_vector)
49
ci = cc
50
else
51
ci = cc * prod ((1 - dis_vector[i]) / (1 - cc) for i in eachindex(p))
52
end
53
return ci
54
end

function
crispy(x, y, q, p, w, v, lambda)
57
relation = 0
58
if credibility(x, y, q, p, w, v) >= lambda && credibility(y, x, q, p, w, v)
>= lambda
59
relation = 1 # x and y are
indifferent
==PAGE:120==
108
B.3. ELECTRE TRI PROCEDURES
60
#println (" They are
indifferent ")
61
elseif
credibility(x, y, q, p, w, v) >= lambda && credibility(y, x, q, p, w
, v) < lambda
62
relation = 2 # x is strictly
preferred to y
63
#println ("x is strictly
preferred to y")
64
elseif
credibility(x, y, q, p, w, v) < lambda && credibility(y, x, q, p, w,
v) >= lambda
65
relation = 3 # y is strictly
preferred to x
66
#println ("y is strictly
preferred to x")
67
else
68
relation = 4 # The two are
incomparable
69
#println (" They are
incomparable ")
70
end
71
return
relation
72
end

Listing B.10: Procedures for categorizing the solutions with ELECTRE-TRI method.
B.4
Synchronous NIMBUS procedures
The synchronous NIMBUS procedures are listed below. These include extracting nadir and ideal
points, adding the MIP model constraints, all scalarizing subproblems, and a procedure to run
all subproblems in parallel.
